	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Qing dynasty</h1>
	
	<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
		<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
		<div id="contentSub"><span class="mw-redirectedfrom">&#160;&#160;(Redirected from <a href="/w/index.php?title=Qing_Dynasty&amp;redirect=no" class="mw-redirect" title="Qing Dynasty">Qing Dynasty</a>)</span></div>
		
		
		
		<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
		<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Not to be confused with the <a href="/wiki/Qin_dynasty" title="Qin dynasty">Qin dynasty</a>, the first dynasty of Imperial China.</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">"Qing" redirects here. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Qing_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Qing (disambiguation)">Qing (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Former empire in Eastern Asia, last imperial regime of China</div>

</p>

<p>The <b>Qing dynasty</b>, officially the <b>Great Qing</b> (<span class="IPA" style="white-space:nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Mandarin" title="Help:IPA/Mandarin">[tɕʰi&#769;ŋ]</a></span>), was the last <a href="/wiki/History_of_China#Imperial_China" title="History of China">imperial</a> <a href="/wiki/Dynasties_in_Chinese_history" title="Dynasties in Chinese history">dynasty</a> of China. It was established in 1636, and ruled <a href="/wiki/China_proper" title="China proper">China proper</a> from 1644 to 1911. It was preceded by the <a href="/wiki/Ming_dynasty" title="Ming dynasty">Ming dynasty</a> and succeeded by the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_China_(1912%E2%80%931949)" title="Republic of China (1912–1949)">Republic of China</a>. The Qing multi-cultural empire lasted for almost three centuries and formed the territorial base for modern China. It was the <a href="/wiki/List_of_largest_empires#Empires_at_their_greatest_extent" title="List of largest empires">fifth largest empire</a> in world history. The dynasty was founded by the <a href="/wiki/Manchu_people" title="Manchu people">Manchu</a> <a href="/wiki/Aisin_Gioro" title="Aisin Gioro">Aisin Gioro</a> clan in <a href="/wiki/Manchuria" title="Manchuria">Manchuria</a>. In the late sixteenth century, <a href="/wiki/Nurhaci" title="Nurhaci">Nurhaci</a>, originally a Ming <a href="/wiki/Jianzhou_Guard" class="mw-redirect" title="Jianzhou Guard">Jianzhou Guard</a> vassal, began organizing "<a href="/wiki/Eight_Banners" title="Eight Banners">Banners</a>", military-social units that included <a href="/wiki/Manchu_people" title="Manchu people">Manchu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Han_Chinese" title="Han Chinese">Han</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mongols" title="Mongols">Mongol</a> elements. Nurhaci formed the Manchu clans into a unified entity and officially proclaimed the <a href="/wiki/Later_Jin_(1616%E2%80%931636)" title="Later Jin (1616–1636)">Later Jin</a> in 1616. By 1636, his son <a href="/wiki/Hong_Taiji" title="Hong Taiji">Hong Taiji</a> began driving Ming forces out of the <a href="/wiki/Liaodong_Peninsula" title="Liaodong Peninsula">Liaodong Peninsula</a> and declared a new dynasty, the Qing.
</p><p>In an unrelated development, peasant rebels led by <a href="/wiki/Li_Zicheng" title="Li Zicheng">Li Zicheng</a> conquered the Ming capital, Beijing, in 1644. Rather than serve them, Ming general <a href="/wiki/Wu_Sangui" title="Wu Sangui">Wu Sangui</a> made an alliance with the Manchus and opened the <a href="/wiki/Shanhai_Pass" title="Shanhai Pass">Shanhai Pass</a> to the Banner Armies led by the regent Prince <a href="/wiki/Dorgon" title="Dorgon">Dorgon</a>. He <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Shanhai_Pass" title="Battle of Shanhai Pass">defeated the rebels</a> and seized the capital. Resistance from the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Ming" title="Southern Ming">Southern Ming</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Revolt_of_the_Three_Feudatories" title="Revolt of the Three Feudatories">Revolt of the Three Feudatories</a> led by Wu Sangui delayed the complete <a href="/wiki/Qing_conquest_of_the_Ming" class="mw-redirect" title="Qing conquest of the Ming">Qing conquest of China proper</a> by nearly four decades. The conquest was only completed in 1683 under the <a href="/wiki/Kangxi_Emperor" title="Kangxi Emperor">Kangxi Emperor</a> (1661–1722). The <a href="/wiki/Ten_Great_Campaigns" title="Ten Great Campaigns">Ten Great Campaigns</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Qianlong_Emperor" title="Qianlong Emperor">Qianlong Emperor</a> from the 1750s to the 1790s <a href="/wiki/Qing_dynasty_in_Inner_Asia" title="Qing dynasty in Inner Asia">extended Qing control</a> into <a href="/wiki/Inner_Asia" title="Inner Asia">Inner Asia</a>. During the peak of the Qing dynasty, the empire ruled over the entirety of today's Mainland China, Hainan, <a href="/wiki/Taiwan_under_Qing_rule" title="Taiwan under Qing rule">Taiwan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mongolia_under_Qing_rule" title="Mongolia under Qing rule">Mongolia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Outer_Manchuria" title="Outer Manchuria">Outer Manchuria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Northwest_China#Outer_Northwest_China" title="Northwest China">Outer Northwest China</a>. 
The early Qing rulers maintained their Manchu customs, and while their title was Emperor, they used "Bogd <a href="/wiki/Khan_(title)" title="Khan (title)">khaan</a>" when dealing with the Mongols and they were patrons of <a href="/wiki/Tibetan_Buddhism" title="Tibetan Buddhism">Tibetan Buddhism</a>. They governed using Confucian styles and institutions of bureaucratic government and retained the <a href="/wiki/Imperial_examination" title="Imperial examination">imperial examinations</a> to recruit Han Chinese to work under or in parallel with Manchus. They also adapted the ideals of the <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Chinese_tributary_system" class="mw-redirect" title="Imperial Chinese tributary system">Chinese tributary system</a> in asserting superiority over neighboring territories such as Korea.
</p><p>The high point of the dynasty came in the late 18th century. By 1800 it steadily deteriorated into poverty and rebellion because of massive internal revolts, heavy foreign pressure, excessive population growth and too-slow adaptation of its governance and its technology. The population rose to some 400 million, but taxes and government revenues were fixed at a low rate, leading to fiscal crisis. Corruption set in, rebels tested government legitimacy, and ruling elites failed to change their mindsets in the face of changes in the world system. Following the <a href="/wiki/Opium_Wars" title="Opium Wars">Opium Wars</a>, European powers led by Great Britain imposed "<a href="/wiki/Unequal_treaty" title="Unequal treaty">unequal treaties</a>", <a href="/wiki/Free_trade" title="Free trade">free trade</a>, <a href="/wiki/Extraterritoriality" title="Extraterritoriality">extraterritoriality</a> and <a href="/wiki/Treaty_ports" title="Treaty ports">treaty ports</a> under foreign control. The <a href="/wiki/Taiping_Rebellion" title="Taiping Rebellion">Taiping Rebellion</a> (1850–1864) and the <a href="/wiki/Dungan_Revolt_(1862%E2%80%9377)" title="Dungan Revolt (1862–77)">Dungan Revolt</a> (1862–1877) in Central Asia led to the deaths of some 20 million people, most of them due to famines caused by war. In spite of these disasters, in the <a href="/wiki/Tongzhi_Restoration" title="Tongzhi Restoration">Tongzhi Restoration</a> of the 1860s, Han Chinese elites rallied to the defense of the Confucian order and the Manchu rulers. The initial gains in the <a href="/wiki/Self-Strengthening_Movement" title="Self-Strengthening Movement">Self-Strengthening Movement</a> were lost in the <a href="/wiki/First_Sino-Japanese_War" title="First Sino-Japanese War">First Sino-Japanese War</a> of 1895, in which the Qing lost its influence over Korea and the <a href="/wiki/Taiwan_under_Qing_rule" title="Taiwan under Qing rule">possession of Taiwan</a>. <a href="/wiki/New_Armies" class="mw-redirect" title="New Armies">New Armies</a> were organized, but the ambitious <a href="/wiki/Hundred_Days%27_Reform" title="Hundred Days&#39; Reform">Hundred Days' Reform</a> of 1898 was turned back in <a href="/wiki/Wuxu_Coup" class="mw-redirect" title="Wuxu Coup">a coup</a> by the conservative <a href="/wiki/Empress_Dowager_Cixi" title="Empress Dowager Cixi">Empress Dowager Cixi</a> (1835–1908), who was in control of the national government (with one interruption) after 1861.  When the <a href="/wiki/Scramble_for_Concessions" class="mw-redirect" title="Scramble for Concessions">Scramble for Concessions</a> by foreign powers triggered the violently anti-foreign "<a href="/wiki/Y%C3%ACh%C3%A9qu%C3%A1n" class="mw-redirect" title="Yìhéquán">Boxers</a>" in 1900, with many foreigners and Christians killed, the <a href="/wiki/Eight-Nation_Alliance" title="Eight-Nation Alliance">foreign powers</a> invaded China.  Cixi sided with the Boxers and was decisively defeated by the eight invading powers, leading to the flight of the Imperial Court to Xi'an.
</p><p>After agreeing to sign the <a href="/wiki/Boxer_Protocol" title="Boxer Protocol">Boxer Protocol</a>, the government initiated unprecedented <a href="/wiki/New_Policies" title="New Policies">fiscal and administrative reforms</a>, including elections, a new legal code, and abolition of the examination system. <a href="/wiki/Sun_Yat-sen" title="Sun Yat-sen">Sun Yat-sen</a> and other revolutionaries competed with constitutional monarchists such as <a href="/wiki/Kang_Youwei" title="Kang Youwei">Kang Youwei</a> and <a href="/wiki/Liang_Qichao" title="Liang Qichao">Liang Qichao</a> to transform the Qing Empire into a modern nation. After the deaths of Cixi and the <a href="/wiki/Guangxu_Emperor" title="Guangxu Emperor">Guangxu Emperor</a> in 1908, the hardline Manchu court alienated reformers and local elites alike by obstructing social reform. The <a href="/wiki/Wuchang_Uprising" title="Wuchang Uprising">Wuchang Uprising</a> on 11 October 1911, led to the <a href="/wiki/Xinhai_Revolution" title="Xinhai Revolution">Xinhai Revolution</a>. General <a href="/wiki/Yuan_Shikai" title="Yuan Shikai">Yuan Shikai</a> negotiated the abdication of <a href="/wiki/Puyi" title="Puyi">Puyi</a>, the last emperor, on 12 February 1912. 
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Names">Names</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Names_of_the_Qing_dynasty" title="Names of the Qing dynasty">Names of the Qing dynasty</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Names_of_China" title="Names of China">Names of China</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:442px;"><a href="/wiki/File:%E6%BB%BF%E8%92%99%E6%BC%A2%E5%90%88%E7%92%A7%E6%95%99%E7%A7%91%E6%9B%B8_(%E7%AF%80%E9%8C%84).png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/%E6%BB%BF%E8%92%99%E6%BC%A2%E5%90%88%E7%92%A7%E6%95%99%E7%A7%91%E6%9B%B8_%28%E7%AF%80%E9%8C%84%29.png/440px-%E6%BB%BF%E8%92%99%E6%BC%A2%E5%90%88%E7%92%A7%E6%95%99%E7%A7%91%E6%9B%B8_%28%E7%AF%80%E9%8C%84%29.png" decoding="async" width="440" height="150" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/%E6%BB%BF%E8%92%99%E6%BC%A2%E5%90%88%E7%92%A7%E6%95%99%E7%A7%91%E6%9B%B8_%28%E7%AF%80%E9%8C%84%29.png/660px-%E6%BB%BF%E8%92%99%E6%BC%A2%E5%90%88%E7%92%A7%E6%95%99%E7%A7%91%E6%9B%B8_%28%E7%AF%80%E9%8C%84%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/%E6%BB%BF%E8%92%99%E6%BC%A2%E5%90%88%E7%92%A7%E6%95%99%E7%A7%91%E6%9B%B8_%28%E7%AF%80%E9%8C%84%29.png/880px-%E6%BB%BF%E8%92%99%E6%BC%A2%E5%90%88%E7%92%A7%E6%95%99%E7%A7%91%E6%9B%B8_%28%E7%AF%80%E9%8C%84%29.png 2x" data-file-width="1691" data-file-height="575" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:%E6%BB%BF%E8%92%99%E6%BC%A2%E5%90%88%E7%92%A7%E6%95%99%E7%A7%91%E6%9B%B8_(%E7%AF%80%E9%8C%84).png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The chapter <i>China</i> (中國) in a Chinese, Manchu, and Mongolian languages (trilingual) textbook published during the Qing dynasty; the passage displayed above reads: "Our country China is located in <a href="/wiki/East_Asia" title="East Asia">East Asia</a> ... For 5000 years, culture flourished (in the land of China) ... Since we are Chinese, how can we not love China."</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Nurhaci" title="Nurhaci">Nurhaci</a> declared himself the "Bright Khan" of the <i>Later Jin</i> (lit. "gold") state in honor both of the 12th–13th century <a href="/wiki/Jurchen_people" title="Jurchen people">Jurchen</a> <a href="/wiki/Jin_dynasty_(1115%E2%80%931234)" title="Jin dynasty (1115–1234)">Jin dynasty</a> and of his <a href="/wiki/Aisin_Gioro" title="Aisin Gioro">Aisin Gioro</a> clan (<i>Aisin</i> being <a href="/wiki/Manchu_language" title="Manchu language">Manchu</a> for the Chinese <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E9%87%91" class="extiw" title="wikt:金">金</a> (<i>jīn</i>, "gold")).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEElliott200156_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEElliott200156-6">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> His son <a href="/wiki/Hong_Taiji" title="Hong Taiji">Hong Taiji</a> renamed the dynasty <i>Great Qing</i> in 1636.<sup id="cite_ref-GreatQing_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GreatQing-7">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> There are competing explanations on the meaning of <i>Qīng</i> (lit. "clear" or "pure"). The name may have been selected in reaction to the name of the Ming dynasty (<a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E6%98%8E" class="extiw" title="wikt:明">明</a>), which consists of the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_characters" title="Chinese characters">Chinese characters</a> for "sun" (<a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E6%97%A5" class="extiw" title="wikt:日">日</a>) and "moon" (<a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E6%9C%88" class="extiw" title="wikt:月">月</a>), both associated with the fire element of the <a href="/wiki/Wu_xing" class="mw-redirect" title="Wu xing">Chinese zodiacal system</a>. The character <i>Qīng</i> (<a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E6%B8%85" class="extiw" title="wikt:清">清</a>) is composed of "water" (<a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E6%B0%B5" class="extiw" title="wikt:氵">氵</a>) and "azure" (<a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E9%9D%92" class="extiw" title="wikt:青">青</a>), both associated with the water element. This association would justify the Qing conquest as defeat of fire by water. The water imagery of the new name may also have had Buddhist overtones of perspicacity and enlightenment and connections with the Bodhisattva <a href="/wiki/Manjusri" class="mw-redirect" title="Manjusri">Manjusri</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECrossley1997212–213_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrossley1997212–213-8">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> The Manchu name <i>daicing</i>, which sounds like a phonetic rendering of <i>Dà Qīng</i> or <i>Dai Ching</i>, may in fact have been derived from a <a href="/wiki/Mongolian_language" title="Mongolian language">Mongolian</a> word "<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886223191"/><span class="font-mong" style="display:inline-block; font-weight:normal; font-size: 1.25em; line-height: 1.25em; -webkit-writing-mode: vertical-lr; -o-writing-mode: vertical-lr; -ms-writing-mode: tb-lr; writing-mode: tb-lr; writing-mode: vertical-lr; vertical-align:text-top;">ᠳᠠᠢᠢᠴᠢᠨ</span>, дайчин" that means "warrior". <i>Daicing gurun</i> may therefore have meant "warrior state", a pun that was only intelligible to Manchu and Mongol people. In the later part of the dynasty, however, even the Manchus themselves had forgotten this possible meaning.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEElliott2001p._402,_note_118_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEElliott2001p._402,_note_118-9">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>After conquering "China proper", the Manchus identified their state as "China" (中國, <i>Zhōngguó</i>; "Middle Kingdom"), and referred to it as <i>Dulimbai Gurun</i> in Manchu (<i>Dulimbai</i> means "central" or "middle," <i>gurun</i> means "nation" or "state"). The emperors equated the lands of the Qing state (including present-day Northeast China, Xinjiang, Mongolia, Tibet and other areas) as "China" in both the Chinese and Manchu languages, defining China as a multi-ethnic state, and rejecting the idea that "China" only meant Han areas. The Qing emperors proclaimed that both Han and non-Han peoples were part of "China". They used both "China" and "Qing" to refer to their state in official documents, international treaties (as the Qing was known internationally as "China"<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> or the "Chinese Empire"<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>) and foreign affairs, and "Chinese language" (Manchu: <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886223191"/><span lang="mnc" class="font-mong-mnc" style="display:inline-block; font-weight:normal; font-size: 1.25em; line-height: 1.25em; -webkit-writing-mode: vertical-lr; -o-writing-mode: vertical-lr; -ms-writing-mode: tb-lr; writing-mode: tb-lr; writing-mode: vertical-lr; vertical-align:text-top;">ᡩᡠᠯᡳᠮᠪᠠᡳ<br />ᡤᡠᡵᡠᠨ &#8205;ᡳ<br /> ᠪᡝᡳᡨᡥᡝ</span> <i>Dulimbai gurun i bithe</i>) included Chinese, Manchu, and Mongol languages, and "Chinese people" (中國之人 <i>Zhōngguó zhī rén</i>; Manchu: <i>Dulimbai gurun i niyalma</i>) referred to all subjects of the empire.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZhao2006n_&#91;httpswebarchiveorgweb20140325231543httpswebspaceutexaseduhl4958perspectivesZhao20-20reinventing20chinapdf_4,_7–10,_and_12–14&#93;_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZhao2006n_[httpswebarchiveorgweb20140325231543httpswebspaceutexaseduhl4958perspectivesZhao20-20reinventing20chinapdf_4,_7–10,_and_12–14]-12">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> In the Chinese-language versions of its treaties and its maps of the world, the Qing government used "Qing" and "China" interchangeably.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_the_Qing_dynasty" title="Timeline of the Qing dynasty">Timeline of the Qing dynasty</a> and <a href="/wiki/History_of_foreign_relations_of_China" title="History of foreign relations of China">History of foreign relations of China</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Formation_of_the_Manchu_state">Formation of the Manchu state</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Manchuria_under_Ming_rule" title="Manchuria under Ming rule">Manchuria under Ming rule</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jurchen_unification" title="Jurchen unification">Jurchen unification</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_the_Jurchens#17th_century" title="Timeline of the Jurchens">Timeline of the Jurchens §&#160;17th century</a></div>
<p>The Qing dynasty was founded not by <a href="/wiki/Han_Chinese" title="Han Chinese">Han Chinese</a>, who constitute the majority of the Chinese population, but by a sedentary farming people known as the <a href="/wiki/Manchu_people" title="Manchu people">Jurchen</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Tungusic_peoples" title="Tungusic peoples">Tungusic people</a> who lived around the region now comprising the Chinese provinces of <a href="/wiki/Jilin" title="Jilin">Jilin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Heilongjiang" title="Heilongjiang">Heilongjiang</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbrey2010220_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbrey2010220-14">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> The Manchus are sometimes mistaken for a <a href="/wiki/Nomad" title="Nomad">nomadic</a> people,<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> which they were not.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> 
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Nurhaci">Nurhaci</span></h4>
<p>What was to become the Manchu state was founded by <a href="/wiki/Nurhaci" title="Nurhaci">Nurhaci</a>, the chieftain of a minor Jurchen tribe&#160;&#8211;&#32;the <a href="/wiki/Aisin_Gioro" title="Aisin Gioro">Aisin Gioro</a>&#160;&#8211;&#32;in <a href="/wiki/Jianzhou_Jurchens" title="Jianzhou Jurchens">Jianzhou</a> in the early 17th century. Nurhaci may have spent time in a Chinese household in his youth, and became fluent in Chinese as well as Mongol, and read the Chinese novels <a href="/wiki/Romance_of_the_Three_Kingdoms" title="Romance of the Three Kingdoms">Romance of the Three Kingdoms</a> and <a href="/wiki/Water_Margin" title="Water Margin">Water Margin</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> Originally a vassal of the Ming emperors, Nurhaci embarked on an intertribal feud in 1582 that escalated into a campaign to unify the nearby tribes. By 1616, he had sufficiently consolidated Jianzhou so as to be able to proclaim himself <a href="/wiki/Khan_(title)" title="Khan (title)">Khan</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Later_Jin_(1616%E2%80%931636)" title="Later Jin (1616–1636)">Great Jin</a> in reference to the <a href="/wiki/Jin_dynasty_(1115%E2%80%931234)" title="Jin dynasty (1115–1234)">previous Jurchen dynasty</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbrey2010220–224_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbrey2010220–224-21">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:CEM-36-Regno-di-Nivche-2429.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/CEM-36-Regno-di-Nivche-2429.jpg/250px-CEM-36-Regno-di-Nivche-2429.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="185" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/CEM-36-Regno-di-Nivche-2429.jpg/375px-CEM-36-Regno-di-Nivche-2429.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/CEM-36-Regno-di-Nivche-2429.jpg/500px-CEM-36-Regno-di-Nivche-2429.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1186" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:CEM-36-Regno-di-Nivche-2429.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An Italian map showing the "Kingdom of the <a href="/wiki/Jurchen_people" title="Jurchen people">Nüzhen</a>" or the "<a href="/wiki/Jin_dynasty_(1115%E2%80%931234)" title="Jin dynasty (1115–1234)">Jin</a> Tartars", who "have occupied and are at present ruling China", north of Liaodong and <a href="/wiki/Korea" title="Korea">Korea</a>, published in 1682. <a href="/wiki/Manchuria" title="Manchuria">Manchuria</a> is the homeland of the Manchus, the designation introduced in 1635 for the Jurchen.</div></div></div>
<p>Two years later, Nurhaci announced the "<a href="/wiki/Seven_Grievances" title="Seven Grievances">Seven Grievances</a>" and openly renounced the sovereignty of Ming overlordship in order to complete the unification of those Jurchen tribes still allied with the Ming emperor. After a series of successful battles, he relocated his capital from <a href="/wiki/Xinbin_Manchu_Autonomous_County" title="Xinbin Manchu Autonomous County">Hetu Ala</a> to successively bigger captured Ming cities in Liaodong: first <a href="/wiki/Liaoyang" title="Liaoyang">Liaoyang</a> in 1621, then <a href="/wiki/Shenyang" title="Shenyang">Shenyang</a> (Mukden) in 1625.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEbrey2010220–224_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEbrey2010220–224-21">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>When the Jurchens were reorganized by Nurhaci into the Eight Banners, many Manchu clans were artificially created as a group of unrelated people founded a new Manchu clan (mukun) using a geographic origin name such as a toponym for their hala (clan name).<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> The irregularities over Jurchen and Manchu clan origin led to the Qing trying to document and systematize the creation of histories for Manchu clans, including manufacturing an entire legend around the origin of the Aisin Gioro clan by taking mythology from the northeast.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Relocating his court from Jianzhou to Liaodong provided Nurhaci access to more resources; it also brought him in close contact with the Khorchin Mongol domains on the plains of Mongolia. Although by this time the once-united Mongol nation had long since fragmented into individual and hostile tribes, these tribes still presented a serious security threat to the Ming borders. Nurhaci's policy towards the Khorchins was to seek their friendship and cooperation against the Ming, securing his western border from a powerful potential enemy.<sup id="cite_ref-Bernard_Hung-Kay_Luk_p.25_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bernard_Hung-Kay_Luk_p.25-24">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Furthermore, the Khorchin proved a useful ally in the war, lending the Jurchens their expertise as cavalry archers. To guarantee this new alliance, Nurhaci initiated a policy of inter-marriages between the Jurchen and Khorchin nobilities, while those who resisted were met with military action. This is a typical example of Nurhaci's initiatives that eventually became official Qing government policy. During most of the Qing period, the Mongols gave military assistance to the Manchus.<sup id="cite_ref-Bernard_Hung-Kay_Luk_p.25_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bernard_Hung-Kay_Luk_p.25-24">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Minggunbattle.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Minggunbattle.jpg/220px-Minggunbattle.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="184" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Minggunbattle.jpg/330px-Minggunbattle.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Minggunbattle.jpg/440px-Minggunbattle.jpg 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="501" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Minggunbattle.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Manchu_people" title="Manchu people">Manchu</a> cavalry charging Ming infantry in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Sarhu" class="mw-redirect" title="Battle of Sarhu">battle of Sarhu</a> in 1619</div></div></div>
<p>Some other important contributions by Nurhaci include ordering the creation of a written <a href="/wiki/Manchu_alphabet" title="Manchu alphabet">Manchu script</a>, based on <a href="/wiki/Mongolian_script" title="Mongolian script">Mongolian script</a>, after the earlier <a href="/wiki/Jurchen_script" title="Jurchen script">Jurchen script</a> was forgotten (it had been derived from <a href="/wiki/Khitan_scripts" title="Khitan scripts">Khitan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chinese_characters" title="Chinese characters">Chinese</a>). Nurhaci also created the civil and military administrative system that eventually evolved into the <a href="/wiki/Eight_Banners" title="Eight Banners">Eight Banners</a>, the defining element of Manchu identity and the foundation for transforming the loosely knitted Jurchen tribes into a nation.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>There were too few ethnic Manchus to conquer China proper, so they gained strength by defeating and absorbing Mongols. More importantly, they added Han Chinese to the Eight Banners.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> The Manchus had to create an entire "Jiu Han jun" (Old Han Army) due to the massive number of Han Chinese soldiers who were absorbed into the Eight Banners by both capture and defection. Ming artillery was responsible for many victories against the Manchus, so the Manchus established an artillery corps made out of Han Chinese soldiers in 1641, and the swelling of Han Chinese numbers in the Eight Banners led in 1642 to all Eight Han Banners being created.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> Armies of defected Ming Han Chinese conquered southern China for the Qing.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Han Chinese played a massive role in the Qing conquest of China. Han Chinese Generals who defected to the Manchu were often <a href="/wiki/Heqin#Qing_dynasty" title="Heqin">given women from the Imperial Aisin Gioro family in marriage</a> while the ordinary soldiers who surrendered were often given non-royal Manchu women as wives.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> Jurchen (Manchu) women married Han Chinese in Liaodong.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECrossley201095_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrossley201095-30">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> Manchu Aisin Gioro princesses were also given in marriage to Han Chinese officials' sons.<sup id="cite_ref-Walthall2008_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Walthall2008-31">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Hong_Taiji">Hong Taiji</span></h4>
<p>The unbroken series of Nurhaci's military successes ended in January 1626 when he was defeated by <a href="/wiki/Yuan_Chonghuan" title="Yuan Chonghuan">Yuan Chonghuan</a> while laying siege to <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Ningyuan" title="Battle of Ningyuan">Ningyuan</a>. He died a few months later and was succeeded by his eighth son, <a href="/wiki/Hong_Taiji" title="Hong Taiji">Hong Taiji</a>, who emerged after a short political struggle amongst other contenders to be the new Khan. Although Hong Taiji was an experienced leader and the commander of two Banners at the time of his succession, his reign did not start well on the military front. The Jurchens suffered yet another defeat in 1627 at the hands of Yuan Chonghuan. As before, this defeat was, in part, due to the Ming's newly acquired Portuguese cannons.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mukden_Palace_in_Shenyang.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Mukden_Palace_in_Shenyang.jpg/170px-Mukden_Palace_in_Shenyang.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="255" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Mukden_Palace_in_Shenyang.jpg/255px-Mukden_Palace_in_Shenyang.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Mukden_Palace_in_Shenyang.jpg/340px-Mukden_Palace_in_Shenyang.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2304" data-file-height="3456" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mukden_Palace_in_Shenyang.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Mukden_Palace" title="Mukden Palace">Mukden Palace</a></div></div></div>
<p>To redress the technological and numerical disparity, Hong Taiji in 1634 created his own artillery corps, the <i>ujen cooha</i> (Chinese: <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E9%87%8D" class="extiw" title="wikt:重">重</a><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E8%BB%8D" class="extiw" title="wikt:軍">軍</a>) from among his existing Han troops who cast their own cannons in the European design with the help of defector Chinese metallurgists. One of the defining events of Hong Taiji's reign was the official adoption of the name "Manchu" for the united Jurchen people in November 1635. In 1635, the Manchus' Mongol allies were fully incorporated into a separate Banner hierarchy under direct Manchu command. Hong Taiji conquered the territory north of Shanhai Pass by Ming dynasty and <a href="/wiki/Ligdan_Khan" title="Ligdan Khan">Ligdan Khan</a> in Inner Mongolia. In April 1636, <a href="/wiki/Mongolian_nobility" title="Mongolian nobility">Mongol nobility</a>  of Inner Mongolia, Manchu nobility and the <a href="/wiki/Mandarin_(bureaucrat)" title="Mandarin (bureaucrat)">Han mandarin</a> held the <a href="/wiki/Kurultai" title="Kurultai">Kurultai</a> in Shenyang, recommended khan of Later Jin to be the emperor of Great Qing empire. One of the <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Seal_of_the_Mongols" title="Imperial Seal of the Mongols">Yuan Dynasty's jade seal</a> has also dedicated to the emperor (Bogd Setsen Khan) by nobility.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> When he was said to be presented with the <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Seal_of_China" class="mw-redirect" title="Imperial Seal of China">imperial seal</a> of the Yuan dynasty after the defeat of the last <a href="/wiki/Khagan" title="Khagan">Khagan</a> of the Mongols, Hong Taiji renamed his state from "Great Jin" to "Great Qing" and elevated his position from Khan to <a href="/wiki/Emperor_of_China" title="Emperor of China">Emperor</a>, suggesting imperial ambitions beyond unifying the Manchu territories. Hong Taiji then proceeded in 1636 to <a href="/wiki/Second_Manchu_invasion_of_Korea" class="mw-redirect" title="Second Manchu invasion of Korea">invade Korea</a> again.
</p><p>The change of the name from Jurchen to Manchu was made to hide the fact that the ancestors of the Manchus, the Jianzhou Jurchens, were ruled by the Chinese.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> The Qing dynasty carefully hid the original editions of the books of "<i>Qing Taizu Wu Huangdi Shilu</i>" and the "<i>Manzhou Shilu Tu</i>" (Taizu Shilu Tu) in the Qing palace, forbidden from public view because they showed that the Manchu Aisin Gioro family had been ruled by the Ming dynasty and followed many Manchu customs that seemed "uncivilized" in later eyes.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> In the Ming period, the Koreans of <a href="/wiki/Joseon" title="Joseon">Joseon</a> referred to the Jurchen inhabited lands north of the Korean peninsula, above the rivers Yalu and Tumen to be part of Ming China, as the "superior country" (sangguk) which they called Ming China.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> The Qing deliberately excluded references and information that showed the Jurchens (Manchus) as subservient to the Ming dynasty, from the <a href="/wiki/History_of_Ming" title="History of Ming">History of Ming</a> to hide their former subservient relationship to the Ming. The <a href="/wiki/Veritable_Records_of_Ming" class="mw-redirect" title="Veritable Records of Ming">Veritable Records of Ming</a> were not used to source content on Jurchens during Ming rule in the History of Ming because of this.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>After the <a href="/wiki/Second_Manchu_invasion_of_Korea" class="mw-redirect" title="Second Manchu invasion of Korea">Second Manchu invasion of Korea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Joseon" title="Joseon">Joseon</a> Korea was forced to give several of their royal princesses as concubines to the Qing Manchu regent Prince <a href="/wiki/Dorgon" title="Dorgon">Dorgon</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> In 1650, Dorgon married the Korean <a href="/wiki/Princess_Uisun" title="Princess Uisun">Princess Uisun</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>This was followed by the creation of the first two Han Banners in 1637 (increasing to eight in 1642). Together these military reforms enabled Hong Taiji to resoundingly defeat Ming forces in a <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Songjin" class="mw-redirect" title="Battle of Songjin">series of battles</a> from 1640 to 1642 for the territories of <a href="/wiki/Songshan,_Liaoning" title="Songshan, Liaoning">Songshan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jinzhou" title="Jinzhou">Jinzhou</a>. This final victory resulted in the surrender of many of the Ming dynasty's most battle-hardened troops, the death of Yuan Chonghuan at the hands of the <a href="/wiki/Chongzhen_Emperor" title="Chongzhen Emperor">Chongzhen Emperor</a> (who thought Yuan had betrayed him), and the complete and permanent withdrawal of the remaining Ming forces north of the <a href="/wiki/Great_Wall_of_China" title="Great Wall of China">Great Wall</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sura_han_ni_chiha._Currency_of_the_farther_East._No.850.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Sura_han_ni_chiha._Currency_of_the_farther_East._No.850.jpg/330px-Sura_han_ni_chiha._Currency_of_the_farther_East._No.850.jpg" decoding="async" width="330" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Sura_han_ni_chiha._Currency_of_the_farther_East._No.850.jpg/495px-Sura_han_ni_chiha._Currency_of_the_farther_East._No.850.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/d9/Sura_han_ni_chiha._Currency_of_the_farther_East._No.850.jpg 2x" data-file-width="520" data-file-height="260" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sura_han_ni_chiha._Currency_of_the_farther_East._No.850.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Sura han ni chiha</i> (Coins of <a href="/wiki/Hong_Taiji" title="Hong Taiji">Tiancong Khan</a>) in <a href="/wiki/Manchu_alphabet" title="Manchu alphabet">Manchu alphabet</a></div></div></div>
<p>Meanwhile, Hong Taiji set up a rudimentary bureaucratic system based on the Ming model. He established six boards or executive level ministries in 1631 to oversee finance, personnel, rites, military, punishments, and public works. However, these administrative organs had very little role initially, and it was not until the eve of completing the conquest ten years later that they fulfilled their government roles.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELi200260–62_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELi200260–62-40">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Hong Taiji's bureaucracy was staffed with many Han Chinese, including many newly surrendered Ming officials. The Manchus' continued dominance was ensured by an ethnic quota for top bureaucratic appointments. Hong Taiji's reign also saw a fundamental change of policy towards his Han Chinese subjects. Nurhaci had treated Han in Liaodong differently according to how much grain they had: those with less than 5 to 7 sin were treated badly, while those with more than that amount were rewarded with property. Due to a revolt by Han in Liaodong in 1623, Nurhaci, who previously gave concessions to conquered Han subjects in Liaodong, turned against them and ordered that they no longer be trusted. He enacted discriminatory policies and killings against them, while ordering that Han who assimilated to the Jurchen (in Jilin) before 1619 be treated equally, as Jurchens were, and not like the conquered Han in Liaodong. Hong Taiji recognized that the Manchus needed to attract Han Chinese, explaining to reluctant Manchus why he needed to treat the Ming defector General <a href="/wiki/Hong_Chengchou" title="Hong Chengchou">Hong Chengchou</a> leniently.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> Hong Taiji instead incorporated them into the Jurchen "nation" as full (if not first-class) citizens, obligated to provide military service. By 1648, less than one-sixth of the bannermen were of Manchu ancestry.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> This change of policy not only increased Hong Taiji's manpower and reduced his military dependence on banners not under his personal control, it also greatly encouraged other Han Chinese subjects of the Ming dynasty to surrender and accept Jurchen rule when they were defeated militarily. Through these and other measures Hong Taiji was able to centralize power unto the office of the Khan, which in the long run prevented the Jurchen federation from fragmenting after his death.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Claiming_the_Mandate_of_Heaven">Claiming the Mandate of Heaven</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Transition_from_Ming_to_Qing" title="Transition from Ming to Qing">Transition from Ming to Qing</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Dorgon,_the_Prince_Rui_(17th_century).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Dorgon%2C_the_Prince_Rui_%2817th_century%29.jpg/170px-Dorgon%2C_the_Prince_Rui_%2817th_century%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="233" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Dorgon%2C_the_Prince_Rui_%2817th_century%29.jpg/255px-Dorgon%2C_the_Prince_Rui_%2817th_century%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Dorgon%2C_the_Prince_Rui_%2817th_century%29.jpg/340px-Dorgon%2C_the_Prince_Rui_%2817th_century%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="700" data-file-height="958" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Dorgon,_the_Prince_Rui_(17th_century).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Dorgon" title="Dorgon">Dorgon</a> (1612–1650)</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Pine,_Plum_and_Cranes.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Pine%2C_Plum_and_Cranes.jpg/170px-Pine%2C_Plum_and_Cranes.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="332" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Pine%2C_Plum_and_Cranes.jpg/255px-Pine%2C_Plum_and_Cranes.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Pine%2C_Plum_and_Cranes.jpg/340px-Pine%2C_Plum_and_Cranes.jpg 2x" data-file-width="700" data-file-height="1366" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Pine,_Plum_and_Cranes.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Pine, Plum and Cranes</i>, 1759, by Shen Quan (1682–1760). Hanging scroll, ink and colour on silk. The <a href="/wiki/Forbidden_City" title="Forbidden City">Palace Museum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Beijing" title="Beijing">Beijing</a></div></div></div>
<p>Hong Taiji died suddenly in September 1643. As the Jurchens had traditionally "elected" their leader through a council of nobles, the Qing state did not have a clear succession system. The leading contenders for power were Hong Taiji's oldest son <a href="/wiki/Hooge_(prince)" class="mw-redirect" title="Hooge (prince)">Hooge</a> and Hong Taiji's half brother <a href="/wiki/Dorgon" title="Dorgon">Dorgon</a>. A compromise installed Hong Taiji's five-year-old son, Fulin, as the <a href="/wiki/Shunzhi_Emperor" title="Shunzhi Emperor">Shunzhi Emperor</a>, with Dorgon as regent and de facto leader of the Manchu nation.
</p><p>Meanwhile, Ming government officials fought against each other, against fiscal collapse, and against a series of <a href="/wiki/Ming_dynasty#Rebellion,_invasion,_collapse" title="Ming dynasty">peasant rebellions</a>. They were unable to capitalise on the Manchu succession dispute and the presence of a minor as emperor.  In April 1644, the capital, <a href="/wiki/Beijing" title="Beijing">Beijing</a>, was sacked by a coalition of rebel forces led by <a href="/wiki/Li_Zicheng" title="Li Zicheng">Li Zicheng</a>, a former minor Ming official, who established a short-lived <a href="/wiki/Shun_dynasty" title="Shun dynasty">Shun dynasty</a>. The last Ming ruler, the <a href="/wiki/Chongzhen_Emperor" title="Chongzhen Emperor">Chongzhen Emperor</a>, committed suicide when the city fell to the rebels, marking the official end of the dynasty.
</p><p>Li Zicheng then led a collection of rebel forces numbering some 200,000<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> to confront <a href="/wiki/Wu_Sangui" title="Wu Sangui">Wu Sangui</a>, the general commanding the Ming garrison at <a href="/wiki/Shanhai_Pass" title="Shanhai Pass">Shanhai Pass</a>, a key pass of the <a href="/wiki/Great_Wall_of_China" title="Great Wall of China">Great Wall</a>, located fifty miles northeast of Beijing, which defended the capital. Wu Sangui, caught between a rebel army twice his size and an enemy he had fought for years, cast his lot with the foreign but familiar Manchus. Wu Sangui may have been influenced by Li Zicheng's mistreatment of wealthy and cultured officials, including Li's own family; it was said that Li took Wu's concubine <a href="/wiki/Chen_Yuanyuan" title="Chen Yuanyuan">Chen Yuanyuan</a> for himself. Wu and Dorgon allied in the name of avenging the death of the <a href="/wiki/Chongzhen_Emperor" title="Chongzhen Emperor">Chongzhen Emperor</a>. Together, the two former enemies met and defeated Li Zicheng's rebel forces in <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Shanhai_Pass" title="Battle of Shanhai Pass">battle on May 27, 1644</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpence201232_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpence201232-44">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The newly allied armies captured Beijing on 6 June. The <a href="/wiki/Shunzhi_Emperor" title="Shunzhi Emperor">Shunzhi Emperor</a> was invested as the "<a href="/wiki/Son_of_Heaven" title="Son of Heaven">Son of Heaven</a>" on 30 October. The Manchus, who had positioned themselves as political heirs to the Ming emperor by defeating Li Zicheng, completed the symbolic transition by holding a formal funeral for the Chongzhen Emperor. However, conquering the rest of China Proper took another seventeen years of battling Ming loyalists, <a href="/wiki/Pretender" title="Pretender">pretenders</a> and rebels. The last Ming pretender, <a href="/wiki/Zhu_Youlang,_Prince_of_Gui" class="mw-redirect" title="Zhu Youlang, Prince of Gui">Prince Gui</a>, sought refuge with the King of <a href="/wiki/Burma" class="mw-redirect" title="Burma">Burma</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pindale_Min" title="Pindale Min">Pindale Min</a>, but was turned over to a Qing expeditionary army commanded by Wu Sangui, who had him brought back to <a href="/wiki/Yunnan" title="Yunnan">Yunnan</a> province and executed in early 1662.
</p><p>The Qing had taken shrewd advantage of Ming civilian government discrimination against the military and encouraged the Ming military to defect by spreading the message that the Manchus valued their skills.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> Banners made up of Han Chinese who defected before 1644 were classed among the Eight Banners, giving them social and legal privileges in addition to being acculturated to Manchu traditions. Han defectors swelled the ranks of the Eight Banners so greatly that ethnic Manchus became a minority—only 16% in 1648, with Han Bannermen dominating at 75% and Mongol Bannermen making up the rest.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENaquinRawski1987141_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENaquinRawski1987141-46">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> Gunpowder weapons like muskets and artillery were wielded by the Chinese Banners.<sup id="cite_ref-books.google.com_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-books.google.com-47">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> Normally, Han Chinese defector troops were deployed as the vanguard, while Manchu Bannermen acted as reserve forces or in the rear and were used predominantly for quick strikes with maximum impact, so as to minimize ethnic Manchu losses.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>This multi-ethnic force conquered China for the Qing.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> The three Liaodong Han Bannermen officers who played key roles in the conquest of southern China were Shang Kexi, Geng Zhongming, and Kong Youde, who governed southern China autonomously as viceroys for the Qing after the conquest.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> Han Chinese Bannermen made up the majority of governors in the early Qing, and they governed and administered China after the conquest, stabilizing Qing rule.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> Han Bannermen dominated the post of governor-general in the time of the Shunzhi and Kangxi Emperors, and also the post of governor, largely excluding ordinary Han civilians from these posts.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>To promote ethnic harmony, a 1648 decree allowed Han Chinese civilian men to marry Manchu women from the Banners with the permission of the Board of Revenue if they were registered daughters of officials or commoners, or with the permission of their banner company captain if they were unregistered commoners. Later in the dynasty the policies allowing intermarriage were done away with.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The southern cadet branch of <a href="/wiki/Confucius" title="Confucius">Confucius</a>' descendants who held the title <i>Wujing boshi</i> (Doctor of the Five Classics) and 65th generation descendant in the northern branch who held the title <a href="/wiki/Duke_Yansheng" title="Duke Yansheng">Duke Yansheng</a> both had their titles confirmed by the Shunzhi Emperor upon the Qing entry into Beijing on 31 October.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWakeman1986&#91;httpsbooksgooglecombooksid8nXLwSG2O8ACpgPA858_858&#93;_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWakeman1986[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid8nXLwSG2O8ACpgPA858_858]-54">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> The Kong's title of Duke was maintained in later reigns.<sup id="cite_ref-Rawski1998_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rawski1998-55">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Radpaddelsch.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Radpaddelsch.jpg/170px-Radpaddelsch.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="254" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Radpaddelsch.jpg/255px-Radpaddelsch.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Radpaddelsch.jpg/340px-Radpaddelsch.jpg 2x" data-file-width="405" data-file-height="604" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Radpaddelsch.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Chinese <a href="/wiki/Paddle_steamer" title="Paddle steamer">paddle-wheel</a> driven ship from a Qing <a href="/wiki/Encyclopedia" title="Encyclopedia">encyclopedia</a> published in 1726</div></div></div>
<p>The first seven years of the Shunzhi Emperor's reign were dominated by the regent prince Dorgon. Because of his own political insecurity, Dorgon followed Hong Taiji's example by ruling in the name of the emperor at the expense of rival Manchu princes, many of whom he demoted or imprisoned under one pretext or another. Although the period of his regency was relatively short, Dorgon's precedents and example cast a long shadow over the dynasty.
</p><p>First, the Manchus had entered "South of the Wall" because Dorgon responded decisively to Wu Sangui's appeal. Then, after capturing Beijing, instead of sacking the city as the rebels had done, Dorgon insisted, over the protests of other Manchu princes, on making it the dynastic capital and reappointing most Ming officials. Choosing Beijing as the capital had not been a straightforward decision, since no major Chinese dynasty had directly taken over its immediate predecessor's capital. Keeping the Ming capital and bureaucracy intact helped quickly stabilize the regime and sped up the conquest of the rest of the country. Dorgon then drastically reduced the influence of the eunuchs, a major force in the Ming bureaucracy, and directed Manchu women not to <a href="/wiki/Foot_binding" title="Foot binding">bind their feet</a> in the Chinese style.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpence201238_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpence201238-56">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>However, not all of Dorgon's policies were equally popular or as easy to implement. The controversial July 1645 edict (the "haircutting order") forced adult Han Chinese men to shave the front of their heads and comb the remaining hair into the <a href="/wiki/Queue_(hairstyle)" title="Queue (hairstyle)">queue</a> hairstyle which was worn by Manchu men, on pain of death.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWakeman1986646–650_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWakeman1986646–650-57">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> The popular description of the order was: "To keep the hair, you lose the head; To keep your head, you cut the hair."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpence201238_56-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpence201238-56">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> To the Manchus, this policy was a test of loyalty and an aid in distinguishing friend from foe. For the Han Chinese, however, it was a humiliating reminder of Qing authority that challenged traditional Confucian values. The <i><a href="/wiki/Classic_of_Filial_Piety" title="Classic of Filial Piety">Classic of Filial Piety</a></i> (<i>Xiaojing</i>) held that "a person's body and hair, being gifts from one's parents, are not to be damaged". Under the Ming dynasty, adult men did not cut their hair but instead wore it in the form of a top-knot.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWakeman1986648n._183_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWakeman1986648n._183-58">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>  The order triggered strong resistance to Qing rule in <a href="/wiki/Jiangnan" title="Jiangnan">Jiangnan</a><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWakeman1986651–680_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWakeman1986651–680-59">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> and massive killing of Han Chinese. It was Han Chinese defectors who carried out massacres against people refusing to wear the queue. Li Chengdong, a Han Chinese general who had served the Ming but surrendered to the Qing,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFaure2007164_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFaure2007164-60">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> ordered his Han troops to carry out three separate massacres in the city of Jiading within a month, resulting in tens of thousands of deaths. At the end of the third massacre, there was hardly a living person left in this city.<sup id="cite_ref-Ebrey_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ebrey-61">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Jiangyin" title="Jiangyin">Jiangyin</a> also held out against about 10,000 Han Chinese Qing troops for 83 days. When the city wall was finally breached on 9 October 1645, the Han Chinese Qing army led by the Han Chinese Ming defector <a href="/w/index.php?title=Liu_Liangzuo&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Liu Liangzuo (page does not exist)">Liu Liangzuo</a> (劉良佐), who had been ordered to "fill the city with corpses before you sheathe your swords", massacred the entire population, killing between 74,000 and 100,000 people.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> The queue was the only aspect of Manchu culture which the Qing forced on the common Han population. The Qing required people serving as officials to wear Manchu clothing, but allowed non-official Han civilians to continue wearing Hanfu (Han clothing).
</p><p>On 31 December 1650, Dorgon suddenly died during a hunting expedition, marking the official start of the Shunzhi Emperor's personal rule. Because the emperor was only 12 years old at that time, most decisions were made on his behalf by his mother, <a href="/wiki/Empress_Dowager_Xiaozhuang" title="Empress Dowager Xiaozhuang">Empress Dowager Xiaozhuang</a>, who turned out to be a skilled political operator.
</p><p>Although his support had been essential to Shunzhi's ascent, Dorgon had centralised so much power in his hands as to become a direct threat to the throne. So much so that upon his death he was bestowed the extraordinary posthumous title of Emperor Yi (Chinese&#58; <span lang="zh-Hant">義皇帝</span>), the only instance in Qing history in which a Manchu "<a href="/wiki/Prince" title="Prince">prince of the blood</a>" (Chinese&#58; <span lang="zh-Hant">親王</span>) was so honored. Two months into Shunzhi's personal rule, however, Dorgon was not only stripped of his titles, but his corpse was disinterred and mutilated.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;c&#93;</a></sup> to atone for multiple "crimes", one of which was persecuting to death Shunzhi's agnate eldest brother, <a href="/wiki/Hooge_(prince)" class="mw-redirect" title="Hooge (prince)">Hooge</a>. More importantly, Dorgon's symbolic fall from grace also led to the purge of his family and associates at court, thus reverting power back to the person of the emperor. After a promising start, Shunzhi's reign was cut short by his early death in 1661 at the age of twenty-four from <a href="/wiki/Smallpox" title="Smallpox">smallpox</a>. He was succeeded by his third son Xuanye, who reigned as the <a href="/wiki/Kangxi_Emperor" title="Kangxi Emperor">Kangxi Emperor</a>.
</p><p>The Manchus sent Han Bannermen to fight against Koxinga's Ming loyalists in Fujian.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHo2011135_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHo2011135-64">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> They removed the population from coastal areas in order to deprive Koxinga's Ming loyalists of resources. This led to a misunderstanding that Manchus were "afraid of water". Han Bannermen carried out the fighting and killing, casting doubt on the claim that fear of the water led to the coastal evacuation and ban on maritime activities.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHo2011198_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHo2011198-65">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> Even though a poem refers to the soldiers carrying out massacres in Fujian as "barbarians", both Han <a href="/wiki/Green_Standard_Army" title="Green Standard Army">Green Standard Army</a> and Han Bannermen were involved and carried out the worst slaughter.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHo2011206_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHo2011206-66">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> 400,000 Green Standard Army soldiers were used against the Three Feudatories in addition to the 200,000 Bannermen.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHo2011307_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHo2011307-67">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Kangxi_Emperor.27s_reign_and_consolidation"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Kangxi_Emperor's_reign_and_consolidation">Kangxi Emperor's reign and consolidation</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Revolt_of_the_Three_Feudatories" title="Revolt of the Three Feudatories">Revolt of the Three Feudatories</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Portrait_of_the_Kangxi_Emperor_in_Court_Dress.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Portrait_of_the_Kangxi_Emperor_in_Court_Dress.jpg/170px-Portrait_of_the_Kangxi_Emperor_in_Court_Dress.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="269" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Portrait_of_the_Kangxi_Emperor_in_Court_Dress.jpg/255px-Portrait_of_the_Kangxi_Emperor_in_Court_Dress.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Portrait_of_the_Kangxi_Emperor_in_Court_Dress.jpg/340px-Portrait_of_the_Kangxi_Emperor_in_Court_Dress.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1136" data-file-height="1798" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Portrait_of_the_Kangxi_Emperor_in_Court_Dress.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Kangxi_Emperor" title="Kangxi Emperor">Kangxi Emperor</a> (r. 1662–1722)</div></div></div>
<p>The sixty-one year reign of the <a href="/wiki/Kangxi_Emperor" title="Kangxi Emperor">Kangxi Emperor</a> was the longest of any Chinese emperor. Kangxi's reign is also celebrated as the beginning of an era known as the "High Qing", during which the dynasty reached the zenith of its social, economic and military power. Kangxi's long reign started when he was eight years old upon the untimely demise of his father. To prevent a repeat of <a href="/wiki/Dorgon" title="Dorgon">Dorgon</a>'s dictatorial monopolizing of power during the regency, the Shunzhi Emperor, on his deathbed, hastily appointed four senior cabinet ministers to govern on behalf of his young son. The four ministers – <a href="/wiki/Sonin_(regent)" title="Sonin (regent)">Sonin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ebilun" title="Ebilun">Ebilun</a>, <a href="/wiki/Suksaha" title="Suksaha">Suksaha</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Oboi" title="Oboi">Oboi</a> – were chosen for their long service, but also to counteract each other's influences. Most important, the four were not closely related to the imperial family and laid no claim to the throne. However, as time passed, through chance and machination, Oboi, the most junior of the four, achieved such political dominance as to be a potential threat. Even though Oboi's loyalty was never an issue, his personal arrogance and political conservatism led him into an escalating conflict with the young emperor. In 1669 Kangxi, through trickery, disarmed and imprisoned Oboi – a significant victory for a fifteen-year-old emperor over a wily politician and experienced commander.
</p><p>The early Manchu rulers established two foundations of legitimacy that help to explain the stability of their dynasty. The first was the bureaucratic institutions and the <a href="/wiki/Neo-Confucian" class="mw-redirect" title="Neo-Confucian">neo-Confucian</a> culture that they adopted from earlier dynasties.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERowe200932–33_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERowe200932–33-68">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> Manchu rulers and Han Chinese <a href="/wiki/Scholar-official" title="Scholar-official">scholar-official</a> elites gradually came to terms with each other. The <a href="/wiki/Imperial_exams#Ming_and_Qing_eras" class="mw-redirect" title="Imperial exams">examination system</a> offered a path for ethnic Han to become officials. Imperial patronage of <a href="/wiki/Kangxi_Dictionary" title="Kangxi Dictionary">Kangxi Dictionary</a> demonstrated respect for Confucian learning, while the <a href="/wiki/Sacred_Edict" class="mw-redirect" title="Sacred Edict">Sacred Edict</a> of 1670 effectively extolled Confucian family values. His attempts to discourage Chinese women from <a href="/wiki/Foot_binding" title="Foot binding">foot binding</a>, however, were unsuccessful.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Pacification_of_the_Dzungars.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Pacification_of_the_Dzungars.jpg/220px-Pacification_of_the_Dzungars.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="213" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/82/Pacification_of_the_Dzungars.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="266" data-file-height="257" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Pacification_of_the_Dzungars.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Camp of the Manchu army in <a href="/wiki/Khalkha" class="mw-redirect" title="Khalkha">Khalkha</a> in 1688</div></div></div>
<p>The second major source of stability was the Central Asian aspect of their Manchu identity, which allowed them to appeal to Mongol, Tibetan and Uighur constituents. The ways of the Qing legitimization were different for the Chinese, Mongolian and Tibetan peoples. This contradicted traditional Chinese worldview requiring acculturation of "barbarians". Qing emperors, on the contrary, sought to prevent this in regard to Mongols and Tibetans.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> The Qing used the title of Emperor (Huangdi) in Chinese, while among Mongols the Qing monarch was referred to as <a href="/wiki/Khan_(title)" title="Khan (title)">Bogda khan</a> (wise Khan), and referred to as Gong Ma in Tibet.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Qianlong_Emperor" title="Qianlong Emperor">Qianlong Emperor</a> propagated the image of himself as a <a href="/wiki/Chakravartin" class="mw-redirect" title="Chakravartin">Buddhist sage ruler</a>, a patron of <a href="/wiki/Tibetan_Buddhism" title="Tibetan Buddhism">Tibetan Buddhism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Manchu_language" title="Manchu language">Manchu language</a>, the Qing monarch was alternately referred to as either Huwangdi (Emperor) or Khan with no special distinction between the two usages. The Kangxi Emperor also welcomed to his court <a href="/wiki/Society_of_Jesus" title="Society of Jesus">Jesuit</a> missionaries, who had first come to China under the Ming. Missionaries including <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Pereira" title="Thomas Pereira">Tomás Pereira</a>, <a href="/wiki/Martino_Martini" title="Martino Martini">Martino Martini</a>, <a href="/wiki/Johann_Adam_Schall_von_Bell" title="Johann Adam Schall von Bell">Johann Adam Schall von Bell</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_Verbiest" title="Ferdinand Verbiest">Ferdinand Verbiest</a> and <a href="/wiki/Antoine_Thomas" title="Antoine Thomas">Antoine Thomas</a> held significant positions as military weapons experts, mathematicians, cartographers, astronomers and advisers to the emperor. The relationship of trust was however lost in the later <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Rites_controversy" title="Chinese Rites controversy">Chinese Rites controversy</a>.
</p><p>Yet controlling the "<a href="/wiki/Mandate_of_Heaven" title="Mandate of Heaven">Mandate of Heaven</a>" was a daunting task. The vastness of China's territory meant that there were only enough banner troops to garrison key cities forming the backbone of a defense network that relied heavily on surrendered Ming soldiers. In addition, three surrendered Ming generals were singled out for their contributions to the establishment of the Qing dynasty, ennobled as feudal princes (藩王), and given governorships over vast territories in Southern China. The chief of these was <a href="/wiki/Wu_Sangui" title="Wu Sangui">Wu Sangui</a>, who was given the provinces of <a href="/wiki/Yunnan" title="Yunnan">Yunnan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Guizhou" title="Guizhou">Guizhou</a>, while generals <a href="/wiki/Shang_Kexi" title="Shang Kexi">Shang Kexi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Geng_Jingzhong" title="Geng Jingzhong">Geng Jingzhong</a> were given <a href="/wiki/Guangdong" title="Guangdong">Guangdong</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fujian" title="Fujian">Fujian</a> provinces respectively.
</p><p>As the years went by, the three feudal lords and their extensive territories became increasingly autonomous. Finally, in 1673, Shang Kexi petitioned Kangxi for permission to retire to his hometown in <a href="/wiki/Liaodong_Peninsula" title="Liaodong Peninsula">Liaodong</a> province and nominated his son as his successor. The young emperor granted his retirement, but denied the heredity of his fief. In reaction, the two other generals decided to petition for their own retirements to test Kangxi's resolve, thinking that he would not risk offending them. The move backfired as the young emperor called their bluff by accepting their requests and ordering that all three fiefdoms to be reverted to the crown.
</p><p>Faced with the stripping of their powers, Wu Sangui, later joined by Geng Zhongming and by Shang Kexi's son <a href="/wiki/Shang_Zhixin" title="Shang Zhixin">Shang Zhixin</a>, felt they had no choice but to revolt. The ensuing <a href="/wiki/Revolt_of_the_Three_Feudatories" title="Revolt of the Three Feudatories">Revolt of the Three Feudatories</a> lasted for eight years. Wu attempted, ultimately in vain, to fire the embers of south China Ming loyalty by restoring Ming customs, ordering that the resented queues be cut, and declaring himself emperor of a new dynasty. At the peak of the rebels' fortunes, they extended their control as far north as the <a href="/wiki/Yangtze" title="Yangtze">Yangtze River</a>, nearly establishing a divided China. Wu then hesitated to go further north, not being able to coordinate strategy with his allies, and Kangxi was able to unify his forces for a counterattack led by a new generation of Manchu generals. By 1681, the Qing government had established control over a ravaged southern China which took several decades to recover.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpence201248–51_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpence201248–51-72">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Znamenniemantchur.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Znamenniemantchur.jpg/170px-Znamenniemantchur.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="291" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Znamenniemantchur.jpg/255px-Znamenniemantchur.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f3/Znamenniemantchur.jpg 2x" data-file-width="300" data-file-height="514" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Znamenniemantchur.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Eight_Banners" title="Eight Banners">Banners</a> of the 17th century</div></div></div>
<p>Manchu Generals and Bannermen were initially put to shame by the better performance of the Han Chinese <a href="/wiki/Green_Standard_Army" title="Green Standard Army">Green Standard Army</a>. Kangxi accordingly assigned generals Sun Sike, Wang Jinbao, and Zhao Liangdong to crush the rebels, since he thought that Han Chinese were superior to Bannermen at battling other Han people.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> Similarly, in north-western China against Wang Fuchen, the Qing used Han Chinese Green Standard Army soldiers and Han Chinese generals as the primary military forces. This choice was due to the rocky terrain, which favoured infantry troops over cavalry, to the desire to keep Bannermen in reserve, and, again, to the belief that Han troops were better at fighting other Han people. These Han generals achieved victory over the rebels.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> Also due to the mountainous terrain, Sichuan and southern Shaanxi were retaken by the Green Standard Army in 1680, with Manchus participating only in logistics and provisions.<sup id="cite_ref-Di_Cosmo_2007_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Di_Cosmo_2007-75">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> 400,000 Green Standard Army soldiers and 150,000 Bannermen served on the Qing side during the war.<sup id="cite_ref-Di_Cosmo_2007_75-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Di_Cosmo_2007-75">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> 213 Han Chinese Banner companies, and 527 companies of Mongol and Manchu Banners were mobilized by the Qing during the revolt.<sup id="cite_ref-books.google.com_47-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-books.google.com-47">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> 400,000 Green Standard Army soldiers were used against the Three Feudatories besides 200,000 Bannermen.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-76">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Qing forces were crushed by Wu from 1673–1674.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> The Qing had the support of the majority of Han Chinese soldiers and Han elite against the Three Feudatories, since they refused to join Wu Sangui in the revolt, while the Eight Banners and Manchu officers fared poorly against Wu Sangui, so the Qing responded with using a massive army of more than 900,000 Han Chinese (non-Banner) instead of the Eight Banners, to fight and crush the Three Feudatories.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> Wu Sangui's forces were crushed by the Green Standard Army, made out of defected Ming soldiers.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>To extend and consolidate the dynasty's control in Central Asia, the Kangxi Emperor personally led a series of military campaigns against the <a href="/wiki/Dzungar_people" title="Dzungar people">Dzungars</a> in <a href="/wiki/Outer_Mongolia" title="Outer Mongolia">Outer Mongolia</a>. The Kangxi Emperor was able to successfully expel <a href="/wiki/Galdan" class="mw-redirect" title="Galdan">Galdan</a>'s invading forces from these regions, which were then incorporated into the empire. Galdan was eventually killed in the <a href="/wiki/Dzungar%E2%80%93Qing_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Dzungar–Qing War">Dzungar–Qing War</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPerdue2005_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPerdue2005-80">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> In 1683, Qing forces received the surrender of <a href="/wiki/Formosa" class="mw-redirect" title="Formosa">Formosa</a> (Taiwan) from <a href="/wiki/Zheng_Keshuang" title="Zheng Keshuang">Zheng Keshuang</a>, grandson of <a href="/wiki/Koxinga" title="Koxinga">Koxinga</a>, who had conquered Taiwan from the <a href="/wiki/Dutch_Empire" title="Dutch Empire">Dutch</a> colonists as a base against the Qing. Zheng Keshuang was awarded the title "Duke Haicheng" (海澄公) and was inducted into the Han Chinese Plain Red Banner of the Eight Banners when he moved to Beijing. Several Ming princes had accompanied <a href="/wiki/Koxinga" title="Koxinga">Koxinga</a> to Taiwan in 1661–1662, including the Prince of Ningjing <a href="/wiki/Zhu_Shugui" title="Zhu Shugui">Zhu Shugui</a> and Prince Zhu Honghuan (朱弘桓), son of <a href="/wiki/Zhu_Yihai" title="Zhu Yihai">Zhu Yihai</a>, where they lived in the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Tungning" title="Kingdom of Tungning">Kingdom of Tungning</a>. The Qing sent the 17 Ming princes still living on Taiwan in 1683 back to mainland China where they spent the rest of their lives in exile since their lives were spared from execution.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> Winning Taiwan freed Kangxi's forces for series of battles over <a href="/wiki/Albazin" class="mw-redirect" title="Albazin">Albazin</a>, the far eastern outpost of the <a href="/wiki/Tsardom_of_Russia" title="Tsardom of Russia">Tsardom of Russia</a>. Zheng's former soldiers on Taiwan like the rattan shield troops were also inducted into the Eight Banners and used by the Qing <a href="/wiki/Sino-Russian_border_conflicts" title="Sino-Russian border conflicts">against Russian Cossacks at Albazin</a>. The 1689 <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Nerchinsk" title="Treaty of Nerchinsk">Treaty of Nerchinsk</a> was China's first formal treaty with a European power and kept the border peaceful for the better part of two centuries. After Galdan's death, his followers, as adherents to Tibetan Buddhism, attempted to control the choice of the next <a href="/wiki/Dalai_Lama" title="Dalai Lama">Dalai Lama</a>. Kangxi dispatched two armies to <a href="/wiki/Lhasa_(prefecture-level_city)" title="Lhasa (prefecture-level city)">Lhasa</a>, the capital of Tibet, and installed a Dalai Lama sympathetic to the Qing.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpence201262–66_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpence201262–66-82">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>By the end of the 17th century, China was at its greatest height of confidence and political control since the Ming dynasty.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reigns_of_the_Yongzheng_and_Qianlong_emperors">Reigns of the Yongzheng and Qianlong emperors</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Ten_Great_Campaigns" title="Ten Great Campaigns">Ten Great Campaigns</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Yonghe_Gong_sign.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Yonghe_Gong_sign.jpg/220px-Yonghe_Gong_sign.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Yonghe_Gong_sign.jpg/330px-Yonghe_Gong_sign.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Yonghe_Gong_sign.jpg/440px-Yonghe_Gong_sign.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="480" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Yonghe_Gong_sign.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A sign in Mongolian, Tibetan, Chinese and Manchu at the <a href="/wiki/Yonghe_Temple" title="Yonghe Temple">Yonghe</a> monastery in Beijing</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Budala5.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Budala5.jpg/220px-Budala5.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Budala5.jpg/330px-Budala5.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Budala5.jpg/440px-Budala5.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2560" data-file-height="1920" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Budala5.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Putuo_Zongcheng_Temple" title="Putuo Zongcheng Temple">Putuo Zongcheng Temple</a> of <a href="/wiki/Chengde" title="Chengde">Chengde</a>, built in the 18th century during the reign of the <a href="/wiki/Qianlong_Emperor" title="Qianlong Emperor">Qianlong Emperor</a></div></div></div>
<p>The reigns of the <a href="/wiki/Yongzheng_Emperor" title="Yongzheng Emperor">Yongzheng Emperor</a> (r. 1723–1735) and his son, the <a href="/wiki/Qianlong_Emperor" title="Qianlong Emperor">Qianlong Emperor</a> (r. 1735–1796), marked the height of Qing power. During this period, the Qing Empire ruled over 13 million square kilometers of territory. Yet, as the historian Jonathan Spence puts it, the empire by the end of the Qianlong reign was "like the sun at midday". In the midst of "many glories", he writes, "signs of decay and even collapse were becoming apparent".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpence201297,_101_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpence201297,_101-83">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>
