</p><p>After the death of the <a href="/wiki/Kangxi_Emperor" title="Kangxi Emperor">Kangxi Emperor</a> in the winter of 1722, his fourth son, Prince Yong (雍親王), became the Yongzheng Emperor. In the later years of Kangxi's reign, Yongzheng and his brothers had fought, and there were rumours that he had usurped the throne – most of the rumours held that Yongzheng's brother Yingzhen (Kangxi's 14th son) was the real successor of the Kangxi Emperor, and that Yongzheng and his confidant Keduo Long had tampered with the Kangxi's testament on the night when Kangxi died, though there was little evidence for these charges. In fact, his father had trusted him with delicate political issues and discussed state policy with him. When Yongzheng came to power at the age of 45, he felt a sense of urgency about the problems that had accumulated in his father's later years, and he did not need instruction on how to exercise power.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpence201272_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpence201272-84">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> In the words of one recent historian, he was "severe, suspicious, and jealous, but extremely capable and resourceful",<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHsü199035_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHsü199035-85">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> and in the words of another, he turned out to be an "early modern state-maker of the first order".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERowe200968_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERowe200968-86">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Yongzheng moved rapidly. First, he promoted Confucian orthodoxy and reversed what he saw as his father's laxness by cracking down on unorthodox sects and by decapitating an anti-Manchu writer his father had pardoned. In 1723 he outlawed Christianity and expelled Christian missionaries, though some were allowed to remain in the capital.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHsü199035–37_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHsü199035–37-87">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> Next, he moved to control the government. He expanded his father's system of <a href="/wiki/Official_Communications_of_the_Chinese_Empire#Memorials" class="mw-redirect" title="Official Communications of the Chinese Empire">Palace Memorials</a>, which brought frank and detailed reports on local conditions directly to the throne without being intercepted by the bureaucracy, and he created a small <a href="/wiki/Grand_Council_(Qing_dynasty)" title="Grand Council (Qing dynasty)">Grand Council</a> of personal advisors, which eventually grew into the emperor's <i>de facto</i> cabinet for the rest of the dynasty. He shrewdly filled key positions with Manchu and Han Chinese officials who depended on his patronage. When he began to realize that the financial crisis was even greater than he had thought, Yongzheng rejected his father's lenient approach to local landowning elites and mounted a campaign to enforce collection of the land tax. The increased revenues were to be used for "money to nourish honesty" among local officials and for local irrigation, schools, roads, and charity. Although these reforms were effective in the north, in the south and lower Yangzi valley, where Kangxi had wooed the elites, there were long established networks of officials and landowners. Yongzheng dispatched experienced Manchu commissioners to penetrate the thickets of falsified land registers and coded account books, but they were met with tricks, passivity, and even violence. The fiscal crisis persisted.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpence201280–83_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpence201280–83-88">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Battle_of_Oroi-Jalatu.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Battle_of_Oroi-Jalatu.jpg/220px-Battle_of_Oroi-Jalatu.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="134" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Battle_of_Oroi-Jalatu.jpg/330px-Battle_of_Oroi-Jalatu.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Battle_of_Oroi-Jalatu.jpg/440px-Battle_of_Oroi-Jalatu.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1036" data-file-height="631" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Battle_of_Oroi-Jalatu.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Campaign against the <a href="/wiki/Dzungar_Khanate" title="Dzungar Khanate">Dzungars</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Dzungar%E2%80%93Qing_Wars" title="Dzungar–Qing Wars">Qing conquest of Xinjiang</a> between 1755 and 1758</div></div></div>
<p>Yongzheng also inherited diplomatic and strategic problems. A team made up entirely of Manchus drew up the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Kyakhta_(1727)" title="Treaty of Kyakhta (1727)">Treaty of Kyakhta (1727)</a> to solidify the diplomatic understanding with Russia. In exchange for territory and trading rights, the Qing would have a free hand dealing with the situation in Mongolia. Yongzheng then turned to that situation, where the Zunghars threatened to re-emerge, and to the southwest, where local <a href="/wiki/Miao_people" title="Miao people">Miao</a> chieftains resisted Qing expansion. These campaigns drained the treasury but established the emperor's control of the military and military finance.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpence201283,_86_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpence201283,_86-89">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Yongzheng Emperor died in 1735. His 24-year-old son, Prince Bao (寶親王), then became the Qianlong Emperor. Qianlong personally led military campaigns near <a href="/wiki/Xinjiang" title="Xinjiang">Xinjiang</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mongolia" title="Mongolia">Mongolia</a>, putting down revolts and uprisings in <a href="/wiki/Sichuan" title="Sichuan">Sichuan</a> and parts of southern China while expanding control over Tibet.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:LordMacartneyEmbassyToChina1793.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/LordMacartneyEmbassyToChina1793.jpg/220px-LordMacartneyEmbassyToChina1793.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="144" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/LordMacartneyEmbassyToChina1793.jpg/330px-LordMacartneyEmbassyToChina1793.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/LordMacartneyEmbassyToChina1793.jpg/440px-LordMacartneyEmbassyToChina1793.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2484" data-file-height="1624" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:LordMacartneyEmbassyToChina1793.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Lord Macartney saluting the <a href="/wiki/Qianlong_Emperor" title="Qianlong Emperor">Qianlong Emperor</a></div></div></div>
<p>The Qianlong Emperor launched several ambitious cultural projects, including the compilation of the <i><a href="/wiki/Siku_Quanshu" title="Siku Quanshu">Siku Quanshu</a></i>, or <i>Complete Repository of the Four Branches of Literature</i>. With a total of over 3,400 books, 79,000 chapters, and 36,304 volumes, the <i>Siku Quanshu</i> is the largest collection of books in Chinese history. Nevertheless, Qianlong used <a href="/wiki/Literary_Inquisition" class="mw-redirect" title="Literary Inquisition">Literary Inquisition</a> to silence opposition. The accusation of individuals began with the emperor's own interpretation of the true meaning of the corresponding words. If the emperor decided these were derogatory or cynical towards the dynasty, persecution would begin. Literary inquisition began with isolated cases at the time of Shunzhi and Kangxi, but became a pattern under Qianlong's rule, during which there were 53 cases of literary persecution.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Beneath outward prosperity and imperial confidence, the later years of Qianlong's reign were marked by rampant corruption and neglect. <a href="/wiki/Heshen" title="Heshen">Heshen</a>, the emperor's handsome young favorite, took advantage of the emperor's indulgence to become one of the most corrupt officials in the history of the dynasty.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> Qianlong's son, the <a href="/wiki/Jiaqing_Emperor" title="Jiaqing Emperor">Jiaqing Emperor</a> (r. 1796–1820), eventually forced Heshen to commit suicide.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Xu_Yang_-_Commerce_on_the_water.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Xu_Yang_-_Commerce_on_the_water.jpg/220px-Xu_Yang_-_Commerce_on_the_water.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="163" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Xu_Yang_-_Commerce_on_the_water.jpg/330px-Xu_Yang_-_Commerce_on_the_water.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Xu_Yang_-_Commerce_on_the_water.jpg/440px-Xu_Yang_-_Commerce_on_the_water.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3314" data-file-height="2458" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Xu_Yang_-_Commerce_on_the_water.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Commerce on the water, <i><a href="/wiki/Prosperous_Suzhou" title="Prosperous Suzhou">Prosperous Suzhou</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Xu_Yang_(Qing_dynasty)" title="Xu Yang (Qing dynasty)">Xu Yang</a>, 1759</div></div></div>
<p>China also began suffering from mounting overpopulation during this period. Population growth was stagnant for the first half of the 17th century due to civil wars and epidemics, but prosperity and internal stability gradually reversed this trend. The introduction of new crops from the Americas such as the <a href="/wiki/Potato" title="Potato">potato</a> and <a href="/wiki/Peanut" title="Peanut">peanut</a> allowed an improved food supply as well, so that the total population of China during the 18th century ballooned from 100 million to 300&#160;million people. Soon all available farmland was used up, forcing peasants to work ever-smaller and more intensely worked plots. The Qianlong Emperor once bemoaned the country's situation by remarking, "The population continues to grow, but the land does not." The only remaining part of the empire that had arable farmland was <a href="/wiki/Manchuria" title="Manchuria">Manchuria</a>, where the provinces of <a href="/wiki/Jilin" title="Jilin">Jilin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Heilongjiang" title="Heilongjiang">Heilongjiang</a> had been walled off as a Manchu homeland. The emperor decreed for the first time that Han Chinese civilians were forbidden to settle.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEElliott2000617_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEElliott2000617-92">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> Mongols were forbidden by the Qing from crossing the borders of their banners, even into other Mongol Banners, and from crossing into neidi (the Han Chinese 18 provinces) and were given serious punishments if they did in order to keep the Mongols divided against each other to benefit the Qing.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> Mongol pilgrims wanting to leave their banner's borders for religious reasons such as pilgrimage had to apply for passports to give them permission.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Select groups of Han Chinese bannermen were mass transferred into Manchu Banners by the Qing, changing their ethnicity from Han Chinese to Manchu. Han Chinese bannermen of Tai Nikan 台尼堪 (watchpost Chinese) and Fusi Nikan 抚顺尼堪 (Fushun Chinese)<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> backgrounds into the Manchu banners in 1740 by order of the Qing <a href="/wiki/Qianlong_emperor" class="mw-redirect" title="Qianlong emperor">Qianlong emperor</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> It was between 1618-1629 when the Han Chinese from Liaodong who later became the Fushun Nikan and Tai Nikan defected to the Jurchens (Manchus).<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> These Han Chinese origin Manchu clans continue to use their original Han surnames and are marked as of Han origin on <a href="/wiki/List_of_Manchu_clans" title="List of Manchu clans">Qing lists of Manchu clans</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Despite officially prohibiting Han Chinese settlement on the Manchu and Mongol lands, by the 18th century the Qing decided to settle Han refugees from northern China who were suffering from famine, floods, and drought into Manchuria and Inner Mongolia.<sup id="cite_ref-Reardon-Anderson_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reardon-Anderson-102">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> Han Chinese then streamed into Manchuria, both illegally and legally, over the Great Wall and <a href="/wiki/Willow_Palisade" title="Willow Palisade">Willow Palisade</a>. As Manchu landlords desired Han Chinese to rent their land and grow grain, most Han Chinese migrants were not evicted. During the eighteenth century Han Chinese farmed 500,000 hectares of privately owned land in Manchuria and 203,583 hectares of lands that were part of courrier stations, noble estates, and Banner lands. In garrisons and towns in Manchuria Han Chinese made up 80% of the population.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1796, open rebellion broke out by the <a href="/wiki/White_Lotus" title="White Lotus">White Lotus Society</a> against the Qing government. The <a href="/wiki/White_Lotus_Rebellion" title="White Lotus Rebellion">White Lotus Rebellion</a> continued for eight years, until 1804, and marked a turning point in the history of the Qing dynasty.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Rebellion.2C_unrest_and_external_pressure"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Rebellion,_unrest_and_external_pressure">Rebellion, unrest and external pressure</span></h3>
 <div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Destroying_Chinese_war_junks,_by_E._Duncan_(1843).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Destroying_Chinese_war_junks%2C_by_E._Duncan_%281843%29.jpg/220px-Destroying_Chinese_war_junks%2C_by_E._Duncan_%281843%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="144" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Destroying_Chinese_war_junks%2C_by_E._Duncan_%281843%29.jpg/330px-Destroying_Chinese_war_junks%2C_by_E._Duncan_%281843%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Destroying_Chinese_war_junks%2C_by_E._Duncan_%281843%29.jpg/440px-Destroying_Chinese_war_junks%2C_by_E._Duncan_%281843%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1040" data-file-height="681" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Destroying_Chinese_war_junks,_by_E._Duncan_(1843).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>British Steamship destroying Chinese war <a href="/wiki/Junk_(ship)" title="Junk (ship)">junks</a> (E. Duncan) (1843)</div></div></div>
<p>At the start of the dynasty, the <a href="/wiki/Late_Imperial_China" class="mw-redirect" title="Late Imperial China">Chinese empire</a> continued to be the hegemonic power in East Asia. Although there was no formal ministry of foreign relations, the <a href="/wiki/Lifan_Yuan" title="Lifan Yuan">Lifan Yuan</a> was responsible for relations with the Mongol and Tibetans in Central Asia, while the <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Chinese_tributary_system" class="mw-redirect" title="Imperial Chinese tributary system">tributary system</a>, a loose set of institutions and customs taken over from the Ming, in theory governed relations with East and Southeast Asian countries. The <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Nerchinsk" title="Treaty of Nerchinsk">Treaty of Nerchinsk</a> (1689) stabilized relations with Czarist Russia.
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/Jahriyya_revolt" title="Jahriyya revolt">Jahriyya revolt</a> sectarian violence between two suborders of the <a href="/wiki/Naqshbandi" title="Naqshbandi">Naqshbandi</a> Sufis, the Jahriyya Sufi Muslims and their rivals, the Khafiyya Sufi Muslims, led to a Jahriyya Sufi Muslim rebellion which the Qing dynasty in China crushed with the help of the Khafiyya Sufi Muslims.<sup id="cite_ref-LipmanLipman1990_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LipmanLipman1990-105">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Eight_Trigrams_uprising_of_1813" title="Eight Trigrams uprising of 1813">Eight Trigrams uprising of 1813</a> broke out in 1813.
</p><p>However, during the 18th century European empires gradually expanded across the world, as European states developed economies built on maritime trade. The dynasty was confronted with <a href="/wiki/Westphalian_sovereignty" title="Westphalian sovereignty">newly developing concepts of the international system</a> and state to state relations. European trading posts expanded into territorial control in nearby <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> and on the islands that are now <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a>. The Qing response, successful for a time, was to establish the <a href="/wiki/Canton_System" title="Canton System">Canton System</a> in 1756, which restricted maritime trade to that city (modern-day <a href="/wiki/Guangzhou" title="Guangzhou">Guangzhou</a>) and gave monopoly trading rights to <a href="/wiki/Hong_(business)" title="Hong (business)">private Chinese merchants</a>. The <a href="/wiki/British_East_India_Company" class="mw-redirect" title="British East India Company">British East India Company</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Dutch_East_India_Company" title="Dutch East India Company">Dutch East India Company</a> had long before been granted similar monopoly rights by their governments.
</p><p>In 1793, the British East India Company, with the support of the British government, sent a delegation to China under <a href="/wiki/George_Macartney,_1st_Earl_Macartney" title="George Macartney, 1st Earl Macartney">Lord George Macartney</a> in order to open <a href="/wiki/Free_trade" title="Free trade">free trade</a> and put relations on a basis of equality. The imperial court viewed trade as of secondary interest, whereas the British saw maritime trade as the key to their economy. The Qianlong Emperor told Macartney "the kings of the myriad nations come by land and sea with all sorts of precious things", and "consequently there is nothing we lack ..."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETêngFairbank195419_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETêngFairbank195419-106">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Foreign_factory_site.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Foreign_factory_site.jpg/280px-Foreign_factory_site.jpg" decoding="async" width="280" height="120" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Foreign_factory_site.jpg/420px-Foreign_factory_site.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Foreign_factory_site.jpg/560px-Foreign_factory_site.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1868" data-file-height="800" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Foreign_factory_site.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>View of the Canton River, showing the <a href="/wiki/Thirteen_Factories" title="Thirteen Factories">Thirteen Factories</a> in the background, 1850–1855</div></div></div>
<p>Demand in Europe for Chinese goods such as silk, tea, and ceramics could only be met if European companies funneled their limited supplies of silver into China. In the late 1700s, the governments of Britain and France were deeply concerned about the imbalance of trade and the drain of silver. To meet the <a href="/wiki/History_of_opium_in_China" title="History of opium in China">growing Chinese demand for opium</a>, the British East India Company greatly expanded its production in Bengal. Since China's economy was essentially self-sufficient, the country had little need to import goods or raw materials from the Europeans, so the usual way of payment was through silver. The <a href="/wiki/Daoguang_Emperor" title="Daoguang Emperor">Daoguang Emperor</a>, concerned both over the outflow of silver and the damage that opium smoking was causing to his subjects, ordered <a href="/wiki/Lin_Zexu" title="Lin Zexu">Lin Zexu</a> to end the opium trade. Lin confiscated the stocks of opium without compensation in 1839, leading Britain to send a military expedition the following year.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:China_imperialism_cartoon.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/China_imperialism_cartoon.jpg/170px-China_imperialism_cartoon.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="233" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/China_imperialism_cartoon.jpg/255px-China_imperialism_cartoon.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/China_imperialism_cartoon.jpg/340px-China_imperialism_cartoon.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3118" data-file-height="4267" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:China_imperialism_cartoon.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>In this political cartoon, <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland" title="United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland">Britain</a>, <a href="/wiki/German_Empire" title="German Empire">Germany</a>, <a href="/wiki/Russian_Empire" title="Russian Empire">Russia</a>, <a href="/wiki/French_Third_Republic" title="French Third Republic">France</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Empire_of_Japan" title="Empire of Japan">Japan</a> are dividing China</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/First_Opium_War" title="First Opium War">First Opium War</a> revealed the outdated state of the Chinese military. The Qing navy, composed entirely of wooden sailing <a href="/wiki/Junk_(ship)" title="Junk (ship)">junks</a>, was severely outclassed by the modern tactics and firepower of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Navy" title="Royal Navy">British Royal Navy</a>. British soldiers, using advanced muskets and artillery, easily outmanoeuvred and outgunned Qing forces in ground battles. The Qing surrender in 1842 marked a decisive, humiliating blow to China. The <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Nanking" title="Treaty of Nanking">Treaty of Nanjing</a>, the first of the "<a href="/wiki/Unequal_treaty" title="Unequal treaty">unequal treaties</a>", demanded war reparations, forced China to open up the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_Ports" class="mw-redirect" title="Treaty Ports">Treaty Ports</a> of <a href="/wiki/Shamian_Island" class="mw-redirect" title="Shamian Island">Canton</a>, <a href="/wiki/Xiamen" title="Xiamen">Amoy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fuzhou" title="Fuzhou">Fuchow</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ningbo" title="Ningbo">Ningpo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shanghai" title="Shanghai">Shanghai</a> to Western trade and missionaries, and to cede <a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong_Island" title="Hong Kong Island">Hong Kong Island</a> to Britain. It revealed weaknesses in the Qing government and provoked rebellions against the regime. In 1842, the Qing dynasty fought a <a href="/wiki/Sino-Sikh_war" class="mw-redirect" title="Sino-Sikh war">war</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Sikh_Empire" title="Sikh Empire">Sikh Empire</a> (the last independent kingdom of India), resulting in a negotiated peace and a return to the <i><a href="/wiki/Status_quo_ante_bellum" title="Status quo ante bellum">status quo ante bellum</a></i>.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Taiping_Rebellion" title="Taiping Rebellion">Taiping Rebellion</a> in the mid-19th century was the first major instance of <a href="/wiki/Anti-Manchuism" title="Anti-Manchuism">anti-Manchu sentiment</a>. Amid widespread social unrest and worsening famine, the rebellion not only posed the most serious threat towards Qing rulers, it has also been called the "bloodiest civil war of all time"; during its fourteen-year course from 1850 to 1864 between 20 and 30 million people died.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPlatt2012xxii_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPlatt2012xxii-107">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hong_Xiuquan" title="Hong Xiuquan">Hong Xiuquan</a>, a failed <a href="/wiki/Imperial_examination" title="Imperial examination">civil service</a> candidate, in 1851 launched an uprising in <a href="/wiki/Guizhou" title="Guizhou">Guizhou</a> province, and established the <a href="/wiki/Taiping_Heavenly_Kingdom" title="Taiping Heavenly Kingdom">Taiping Heavenly Kingdom</a> with Hong himself as king. Hong announced that he had visions of <a href="/wiki/Jehovah" title="Jehovah">God</a> and that he was the brother of Jesus Christ. Slavery, concubinage, arranged marriage, opium smoking, footbinding, judicial torture, and the worship of idols were all banned. However, success led to internal feuds, defections and corruption. In addition, British and French troops, equipped with modern weapons, had come to the assistance of the Qing imperial army. It was not until 1864 that Qing armies under <a href="/wiki/Zeng_Guofan" title="Zeng Guofan">Zeng Guofan</a> succeeded in crushing the revolt. After the outbreak of this rebellion, there were also revolts by the <a href="/wiki/Hui_people" title="Hui people">Muslims</a> and <a href="/wiki/Miao_people" title="Miao people">Miao people</a> of China against the Qing dynasty, most notably in the <a href="/wiki/Miao_Rebellion_(1854%E2%80%9373)" title="Miao Rebellion (1854–73)">Miao Rebellion (1854–73)</a> in <a href="/wiki/Guizhou" title="Guizhou">Guizhou</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Panthay_Rebellion" title="Panthay Rebellion">Panthay Rebellion</a> (1856–1873) in <a href="/wiki/Yunnan" title="Yunnan">Yunnan</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Dungan_Revolt_(1862%E2%80%9377)" title="Dungan Revolt (1862–77)">Dungan Revolt (1862–77)</a> in the northwest.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Regaining_the_Provincial_Capital_of_Ruizhou.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Regaining_the_Provincial_Capital_of_Ruizhou.jpg/280px-Regaining_the_Provincial_Capital_of_Ruizhou.jpg" decoding="async" width="280" height="123" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Regaining_the_Provincial_Capital_of_Ruizhou.jpg/420px-Regaining_the_Provincial_Capital_of_Ruizhou.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Regaining_the_Provincial_Capital_of_Ruizhou.jpg/560px-Regaining_the_Provincial_Capital_of_Ruizhou.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2000" data-file-height="877" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Regaining_the_Provincial_Capital_of_Ruizhou.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A scene of the Taiping Rebellion, 1850–1864</div></div></div>
<p>The Western powers, largely unsatisfied with the Treaty of Nanjing, gave grudging support to the Qing government during the <a href="/wiki/Taiping_Rebellion" title="Taiping Rebellion">Taiping</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nian_Rebellion" title="Nian Rebellion">Nian Rebellions</a>. China's income fell sharply during the wars as vast areas of farmland were destroyed, millions of lives were lost, and countless armies were raised and equipped to fight the rebels. In 1854, Britain tried to re-negotiate the Treaty of Nanjing, inserting clauses allowing British commercial access to Chinese rivers and the creation of a permanent British embassy at Beijing.
</p><p>In 1856, Qing authorities, in searching for a pirate, boarded a ship, the <i>Arrow</i>, which the British claimed had been flying the British flag, an incident which led to the <a href="/wiki/Second_Opium_War" title="Second Opium War">Second Opium War</a>. In 1858, facing no other options, the <a href="/wiki/Xianfeng_Emperor" title="Xianfeng Emperor">Xianfeng Emperor</a> agreed to the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Tientsin" title="Treaty of Tientsin">Treaty of Tientsin</a>, which contained clauses deeply insulting to the Chinese, such as a demand that all official Chinese documents be written in English and a proviso granting British warships unlimited access to all navigable Chinese rivers.
</p><p>Ratification of the treaty in the following year led to a resumption of hostilities. In 1860, with Anglo-French forces marching on Beijing, the emperor and his court fled the capital for the <a href="/wiki/Chengde_Mountain_Resort" title="Chengde Mountain Resort">imperial hunting lodge at Rehe</a>. Once in Beijing, the Anglo-French forces looted the <a href="/wiki/Old_Summer_Palace" title="Old Summer Palace">Old Summer Palace</a> and, in an act of revenge for the arrest of several Englishmen, burnt it to the ground. <a href="/wiki/Prince_Gong_(Qing_dynasty)" class="mw-redirect" title="Prince Gong (Qing dynasty)">Prince Gong</a>, a younger half-brother of the emperor, who had been left as his brother's proxy in the capital, was forced to sign the <a href="/wiki/Convention_of_Beijing" class="mw-redirect" title="Convention of Beijing">Convention of Beijing</a>. The humiliated emperor died the following year at Rehe.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Self-strengthening_and_the_frustration_of_reforms">Self-strengthening and the frustration of reforms</span></h3>
<p>Yet the dynasty rallied. Chinese generals and officials such as <a href="/wiki/Zuo_Zongtang" title="Zuo Zongtang">Zuo Zongtang</a> led the suppression of rebellions and stood behind the Manchus. When the <a href="/wiki/Tongzhi_Emperor" title="Tongzhi Emperor">Tongzhi Emperor</a> came to the throne at the age of five in 1861, these officials rallied around him in what was called the <a href="/wiki/Tongzhi_Restoration" title="Tongzhi Restoration">Tongzhi Restoration</a>. Their aim was to adopt Western military technology in order to preserve Confucian values. <a href="/wiki/Zeng_Guofan" title="Zeng Guofan">Zeng Guofan</a>, in alliance with Prince Gong, sponsored the rise of younger officials such as <a href="/wiki/Li_Hongzhang" title="Li Hongzhang">Li Hongzhang</a>, who put the dynasty back on its feet financially and instituted the <a href="/wiki/Self-Strengthening_Movement" title="Self-Strengthening Movement">Self-Strengthening Movement</a>. The reformers then proceeded with institutional reforms, including China's first unified ministry of foreign affairs, the <a href="/wiki/Zongli_Yamen" title="Zongli Yamen">Zongli Yamen</a>; allowing foreign diplomats to reside in the capital; establishment of the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Maritime_Customs_Service" title="Chinese Maritime Customs Service">Imperial Maritime Customs Service</a>; the formation of modernized armies, such as the <a href="/wiki/Beiyang_Army" title="Beiyang Army">Beiyang Army</a>, as well as a navy; and the purchase from Europeans of armament factories.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWright1957196–221_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWright1957196–221-108">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_situation_in_the_Far_East_by_Tse_Tsan-tai.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/The_situation_in_the_Far_East_by_Tse_Tsan-tai.jpg/170px-The_situation_in_the_Far_East_by_Tse_Tsan-tai.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="245" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/The_situation_in_the_Far_East_by_Tse_Tsan-tai.jpg/255px-The_situation_in_the_Far_East_by_Tse_Tsan-tai.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/The_situation_in_the_Far_East_by_Tse_Tsan-tai.jpg/340px-The_situation_in_the_Far_East_by_Tse_Tsan-tai.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1245" data-file-height="1794" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_situation_in_the_Far_East_by_Tse_Tsan-tai.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Imperialism 1900: The bear represents Russia, the lion Britain, the frog France, the sun Japan, and the eagle the United States.</div></div></div>
<p>The dynasty lost control of peripheral territories bit by bit. In return for promises of support against the British and the French, the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Empire" title="Russian Empire">Russian Empire</a> took large chunks of territory in the Northeast in 1860. The period of cooperation between the reformers and the European powers ended with the <a href="/wiki/Tientsin_Massacre" class="mw-redirect" title="Tientsin Massacre">Tientsin Massacre</a> of 1870, which was incited by the murder of French nuns set off by the belligerence of local French diplomats. Starting with the <a href="/wiki/Cochinchina_Campaign" title="Cochinchina Campaign">Cochinchina Campaign</a> in 1858, France expanded control of Indochina. By 1883, France was in full control of the region and had reached the Chinese border. The <a href="/wiki/Sino-French_War" title="Sino-French War">Sino-French War</a> began with a surprise attack by the French on the Chinese southern fleet at Fuzhou. After that the Chinese declared war on the French. A <a href="/wiki/Keelung_Campaign" class="mw-redirect" title="Keelung Campaign">French invasion of Taiwan was halted</a> and the French were defeated on land in Tonkin at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Bang_Bo" class="mw-redirect" title="Battle of Bang Bo">Battle of Bang Bo</a>. However Japan threatened to enter the war against China due to the Gapsin Coup and China chose to end the war with negotiations. The war ended in 1885 with the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Tientsin_(1885)" title="Treaty of Tientsin (1885)">Treaty of Tientsin (1885)</a> and the Chinese recognition of the French protectorate in Vietnam.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1884, pro-Japanese Koreans in Seoul led the <a href="/wiki/Gapsin_Coup" title="Gapsin Coup">Gapsin Coup</a>. Tensions between China and Japan rose after China intervened to suppress the uprising. Japanese Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/It%C5%8D_Hirobumi" title="Itō Hirobumi">Itō Hirobumi</a> and Li Hongzhang signed the <a href="/wiki/Convention_of_Tientsin" title="Convention of Tientsin">Convention of Tientsin</a>, an agreement to withdraw troops simultaneously, but the <a href="/wiki/First_Sino-Japanese_War" title="First Sino-Japanese War">First Sino-Japanese War</a> of 1895 was a military humiliation. The <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Shimonoseki" title="Treaty of Shimonoseki">Treaty of Shimonoseki</a> recognized Korean independence and ceded <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Taiwan</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Pescadores" class="mw-redirect" title="Pescadores">Pescadores</a> to Japan. The terms might have been harsher, but when a Japanese citizen attacked and wounded Li Hongzhang, an international outcry shamed the Japanese into revising them. The original agreement stipulated the cession of <a href="/wiki/Liaodong_Peninsula" title="Liaodong Peninsula">Liaodong Peninsula</a> to Japan, but Russia, with its own designs on the territory, along with Germany and France, in what was known as the <a href="/wiki/Triple_Intervention" title="Triple Intervention">Triple Intervention</a>, successfully put pressure on the Japanese to abandon the peninsula.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Empress-Dowager-Cixi1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Empress-Dowager-Cixi1.jpg/170px-Empress-Dowager-Cixi1.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="266" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Empress-Dowager-Cixi1.jpg/255px-Empress-Dowager-Cixi1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Empress-Dowager-Cixi1.jpg/340px-Empress-Dowager-Cixi1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1548" data-file-height="2421" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Empress-Dowager-Cixi1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Painting of Empress Dowager Cixi by Dutch American artist Hubert Vos circa 1905</div></div></div>
<p>These years saw an evolution in the participation of <a href="/wiki/Empress_Dowager_Cixi" title="Empress Dowager Cixi">Empress Dowager Cixi</a> (<a href="/wiki/Wade%E2%80%93Giles" title="Wade–Giles">Wade–Giles</a>: Tz'u-Hsi) in state affairs. She entered the imperial palace in the 1850s as a concubine to the <a href="/wiki/Xianfeng_Emperor" title="Xianfeng Emperor">Xianfeng Emperor</a> (r. 1850–1861) and came to power in 1861 after her five-year-old son, the Tongzhi Emperor ascended the throne. She, the <a href="/wiki/Empress_Dowager_Ci%27an" title="Empress Dowager Ci&#39;an">Empress Dowager Ci'an</a> (who had been Xianfeng's empress), and Prince Gong (a son of the Daoguang Emperor), staged <a href="/wiki/Xinyou_Coup" title="Xinyou Coup">a coup</a> that ousted several regents for the boy emperor. Between 1861 and 1873, she and Ci'an served as regents, choosing the reign title "Tongzhi" (ruling together). Following the emperor's death in 1875, Cixi's nephew, the <a href="/wiki/Guangxu_Emperor" title="Guangxu Emperor">Guangxu Emperor</a>, took the throne, in violation of the dynastic custom that the new emperor be of the next generation, and another regency began. In the spring of 1881, Ci'an suddenly died, aged only forty-three, leaving Cixi as sole regent.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECrossley2010117_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrossley2010117-111">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>From 1889, when Guangxu began to rule in his own right, to 1898, the Empress Dowager lived in semi-retirement, spending the majority of the year at the <a href="/wiki/Summer_Palace" title="Summer Palace">Summer Palace</a>. On 1 November 1897, two German Roman Catholic missionaries were murdered in the southern part of <a href="/wiki/Shandong" title="Shandong">Shandong province</a> (the <a href="/wiki/Juye_Incident" title="Juye Incident">Juye Incident</a>). <a href="/wiki/German_Empire" title="German Empire">Germany</a> used the murders as a pretext for a naval occupation of <a href="/wiki/Jiaozhou_Bay" title="Jiaozhou Bay">Jiaozhou Bay</a>. The occupation prompted a "scramble for concessions" in 1898, which included the <a href="/wiki/Kiautschou_Bay_concession" title="Kiautschou Bay concession">German lease of Jiazhou Bay</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Kwantung_Leased_Territory" title="Kwantung Leased Territory">Russian acquisition of Liaodong</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Convention_for_the_Extension_of_Hong_Kong_Territory" title="Convention for the Extension of Hong Kong Territory">British lease of the New Territories of Hong Kong</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Within_historic_grounds_of_the_Forbidden_City_in_Pekin,_China,_on_November_28_celebrated_the_victory_of_the_Allies.,_ca._-_NARA_-_532582.tif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Within_historic_grounds_of_the_Forbidden_City_in_Pekin%2C_China%2C_on_November_28_celebrated_the_victory_of_the_Allies.%2C_ca._-_NARA_-_532582.tif/lossy-page1-220px-Within_historic_grounds_of_the_Forbidden_City_in_Pekin%2C_China%2C_on_November_28_celebrated_the_victory_of_the_Allies.%2C_ca._-_NARA_-_532582.tif.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="164" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Within_historic_grounds_of_the_Forbidden_City_in_Pekin%2C_China%2C_on_November_28_celebrated_the_victory_of_the_Allies.%2C_ca._-_NARA_-_532582.tif/lossy-page1-330px-Within_historic_grounds_of_the_Forbidden_City_in_Pekin%2C_China%2C_on_November_28_celebrated_the_victory_of_the_Allies.%2C_ca._-_NARA_-_532582.tif.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Within_historic_grounds_of_the_Forbidden_City_in_Pekin%2C_China%2C_on_November_28_celebrated_the_victory_of_the_Allies.%2C_ca._-_NARA_-_532582.tif/lossy-page1-440px-Within_historic_grounds_of_the_Forbidden_City_in_Pekin%2C_China%2C_on_November_28_celebrated_the_victory_of_the_Allies.%2C_ca._-_NARA_-_532582.tif.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3000" data-file-height="2235" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Within_historic_grounds_of_the_Forbidden_City_in_Pekin,_China,_on_November_28_celebrated_the_victory_of_the_Allies.,_ca._-_NARA_-_532582.tif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Eight-Nation_Alliance" title="Eight-Nation Alliance">Foreign armies</a> assemble inside the <a href="/wiki/Forbidden_City" title="Forbidden City">Forbidden City</a> after capturing Beijing, 28 November 1900</div></div></div>
<p>In the wake of these external defeats, the Guangxu Emperor initiated the <a href="/wiki/Hundred_Days%27_Reform" title="Hundred Days&#39; Reform">Hundred Days' Reform</a> of 1898. Newer, more radical advisers such as <a href="/wiki/Kang_Youwei" title="Kang Youwei">Kang Youwei</a> were given positions of influence. The emperor issued a series of edicts and plans were made to reorganize the <a href="/wiki/Bureaucracy" title="Bureaucracy">bureaucracy</a>, restructure the school system, and appoint new officials. Opposition from the bureaucracy was immediate and intense. Although she had been involved in the initial reforms, the Empress Dowager <a href="/wiki/Wuxu_Coup" class="mw-redirect" title="Wuxu Coup">stepped in to call them off</a>, arrested and executed several reformers, and took over day-to-day control of policy. Yet many of the plans stayed in place, and the goals of reform were implanted.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReynolds199335–36_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReynolds199335–36-112">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Widespread drought in North China, combined with the imperialist designs of European powers and the instability of the Qing government, created conditions that led to the emergence of the Righteous and Harmonious Fists, or "<a href="/wiki/Boxer_Rebellion" title="Boxer Rebellion">Boxers</a>." In 1900, local groups of Boxers proclaiming support for the Qing dynasty murdered foreign missionaries and large numbers of Chinese Christians, then converged on Beijing to besiege the Foreign Legation Quarter. A coalition of European, Japanese, and Russian armies (the <a href="/wiki/Eight-Nation_Alliance" title="Eight-Nation Alliance">Eight-Nation Alliance</a>) then entered China without diplomatic notice, much less permission. Cixi declared war on all of these nations, only to lose control of Beijing after a short, but hard-fought campaign. She fled to <a href="/wiki/Xi%27an" title="Xi&#39;an">Xi'an</a>. The victorious allies drew up scores of demands on the Qing government, including compensation for their expenses in invading China and execution of complicit officials.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpence2012223–225_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpence2012223–225-113">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Reform.2C_revolution.2C_collapse"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Reform,_revolution,_collapse">Reform, revolution, collapse</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:YuanShikaiPresidente1915.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/YuanShikaiPresidente1915.jpg/170px-YuanShikaiPresidente1915.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="226" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/YuanShikaiPresidente1915.jpg/255px-YuanShikaiPresidente1915.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/YuanShikaiPresidente1915.jpg/340px-YuanShikaiPresidente1915.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2480" data-file-height="3296" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:YuanShikaiPresidente1915.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Yuan_Shikai" title="Yuan Shikai">Yuan Shikai</a></div></div></div>
<p>By the early 20th century, mass civil disorder had begun in China, and it was growing continuously. To overcome such problems, <a href="/wiki/Empress_Dowager_Cixi" title="Empress Dowager Cixi">Empress Dowager Cixi</a> issued an imperial edict in 1901 calling for reform proposals from the governors-general and governors and initiated the era of the dynasty's "<a href="/wiki/New_Policies" title="New Policies">New Policies</a>", also known as the "Late Qing Reform". The edict paved the way for the most far-reaching reforms in terms of their social consequences, including the creation of a national education system and the abolition of the <a href="/wiki/Imperial_examination" title="Imperial examination">imperial examinations</a> in 1905.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKaske2008235_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKaske2008235-114">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Guangxu Emperor died on 14 November 1908, and on 15 November 1908, Cixi also died. Rumors held that she or <a href="/wiki/Yuan_Shikai" title="Yuan Shikai">Yuan Shikai</a> ordered trusted eunuchs to poison the Guangxu Emperor, and an autopsy conducted nearly a century later confirmed lethal levels of arsenic in his corpse.<sup id="cite_ref-mu_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mu-115">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Puyi" title="Puyi">Puyi</a>, the oldest son of <a href="/wiki/Zaifeng,_Prince_Chun" title="Zaifeng, Prince Chun">Zaifeng, Prince Chun</a>, and nephew to the childless Guangxu Emperor, was appointed successor at the age of two, leaving Zaifeng with the regency. This was followed by the dismissal of General Yuan Shikai from his former positions of power. In April 1911 Zaifeng created a cabinet in which there were two vice-premiers. Nonetheless, this cabinet was also known by contemporaries as "The Royal Cabinet" because among the thirteen cabinet members, five were members of the imperial family or <a href="/wiki/Aisin_Gioro" title="Aisin Gioro">Aisin Gioro</a> relatives.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> This brought a wide range of negative opinions from senior officials like <a href="/wiki/Zhang_Zhidong" title="Zhang Zhidong">Zhang Zhidong</a>.
The <a href="/wiki/Wuchang_Uprising" title="Wuchang Uprising">Wuchang Uprising</a> of 10 October 1911 was a success; by November 14 of the 15 provinces had rejected Qing rule. This led to the creation of a new central government, the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_China_(1912%E2%80%931949)" title="Republic of China (1912–1949)">Republic of China</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Nanjing" title="Nanjing">Nanjing</a> with <a href="/wiki/Sun_Yat-sen" title="Sun Yat-sen">Sun Yat-sen</a> as its provisional head. Many provinces soon began "separating" from Qing control. Seeing a desperate situation unfold, the Qing government brought Yuan Shikai back to military power. He took control of his <a href="/wiki/Beiyang_Army" title="Beiyang Army">Beiyang Army</a> to crush the revolution in Wuhan at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Yangxia" title="Battle of Yangxia">Battle of Yangxia</a>. After taking the position of <a href="/wiki/Chancellor_(China)" class="mw-redirect" title="Chancellor (China)">Prime Minister</a> and creating his own cabinet, Yuan Shikai went as far as to ask for the removal of Zaifeng from the regency. This removal later proceeded with directions from <a href="/wiki/Empress_Dowager_Longyu" title="Empress Dowager Longyu">Empress Dowager Longyu</a>. Yuan Shikai was now a dictator--the ruler of China and the Manchu dynasty had lost all power; it formally abdicated in early 1912. 
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Pitched_battle_between_the_imperial_and_revolutionary_army_Wellcome_L0040013.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Pitched_battle_between_the_imperial_and_revolutionary_army_Wellcome_L0040013.jpg/220px-Pitched_battle_between_the_imperial_and_revolutionary_army_Wellcome_L0040013.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="161" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Pitched_battle_between_the_imperial_and_revolutionary_army_Wellcome_L0040013.jpg/330px-Pitched_battle_between_the_imperial_and_revolutionary_army_Wellcome_L0040013.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Pitched_battle_between_the_imperial_and_revolutionary_army_Wellcome_L0040013.jpg/440px-Pitched_battle_between_the_imperial_and_revolutionary_army_Wellcome_L0040013.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4992" data-file-height="3664" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Pitched_battle_between_the_imperial_and_revolutionary_army_Wellcome_L0040013.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A pitched battle between the imperial and revolutionary armies in 1911</div></div></div>
<p>Premier Yuan Shikai and his Beiyang commanders decided that going to war would be unreasonable and costly. Similarly, Sun Yat-sen wanted a republican constitutional reform, for the benefit of China's economy and populace. With permission from Empress Dowager Longyu, Yuan Shikai began negotiating with Sun Yat-sen, who decided that his goal had been achieved in forming a republic, and that therefore he could allow Yuan to step into the position of <a href="/wiki/List_of_Presidents_of_the_Republic_of_China" class="mw-redirect" title="List of Presidents of the Republic of China">President of the Republic of China</a>.
</p><p>On 12 February 1912, after rounds of negotiations, Longyu issued an imperial edict bringing about the <a href="/wiki/Puyi#Abdication" title="Puyi">abdication</a> of the child emperor Puyi. This brought an end to over 2,000 years of <a href="/wiki/Imperial_China" class="mw-redirect" title="Imperial China">Imperial China</a> and began an extended period of instability of <a href="/wiki/Warlord_era" class="mw-redirect" title="Warlord era">warlord factionalism</a>. The unorganized political and economic systems combined with a widespread criticism of <a href="/wiki/Chinese_culture" title="Chinese culture">Chinese culture</a> led to questioning and doubt about the future. Some Qing loyalists organized themselves as "<a href="/wiki/Royalist_Party" title="Royalist Party">Royalist Party</a>", and tried to use <a href="/wiki/Militant" title="Militant">militant</a> activism and open rebellions to restore the monarchy, but to no avail.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBillingsley198856–59_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBillingsley198856–59-117">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> In July 1917, there was an <a href="/wiki/Manchu_Restoration" title="Manchu Restoration">abortive attempt</a> to restore the Qing dynasty led by <a href="/wiki/Zhang_Xun_(Qing_loyalist)" class="mw-redirect" title="Zhang Xun (Qing loyalist)">Zhang Xun</a>, which was quickly reversed by <a href="/wiki/Army_of_the_Republic_of_China" class="mw-redirect" title="Army of the Republic of China">republican troops</a>. In the 1930s, the <a href="/wiki/Empire_of_Japan" title="Empire of Japan">Empire of Japan</a> <a href="/wiki/Japanese_invasion_of_Manchuria" title="Japanese invasion of Manchuria">invaded</a> Northeast China and founded <a href="/wiki/Manchukuo" title="Manchukuo">Manchukuo</a> in 1932, with Puyi as its <a href="/wiki/Emperor_of_Manchukuo" class="mw-redirect" title="Emperor of Manchukuo">emperor</a>. After the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_invasion_of_Manchuria" title="Soviet invasion of Manchuria">invasion</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a>, Manchukuo fell in 1945.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Government">Government</span></h2>
<table class="box-More_citations_needed_section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><a href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" decoding="async" width="50" height="39" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="399" /></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Qing_dynasty&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.<br /><small><span class="plainlinks"><i>Find sources:</i>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?as_eq=wikipedia&amp;q=%22Qing+dynasty%22">"Qing dynasty"</a>&#160;–&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?tbm=nws&amp;q=%22Qing+dynasty%22+-wikipedia">news</a>&#160;<b>·</b> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?&amp;q=%22Qing+dynasty%22+site:news.google.com/newspapers&amp;source=newspapers">newspapers</a>&#160;<b>·</b> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?tbs=bks:1&amp;q=%22Qing+dynasty%22+-wikipedia">books</a>&#160;<b>·</b> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//scholar.google.com/scholar?q=%22Qing+dynasty%22">scholar</a>&#160;<b>·</b> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.jstor.org/action/doBasicSearch?Query=%22Qing+dynasty%22&amp;acc=on&amp;wc=on">JSTOR</a></span></small></span>  <small class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">February 2014</span>)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_emperors_of_the_Qing_dynasty" title="List of emperors of the Qing dynasty">List of emperors of the Qing dynasty</a>, <a href="/wiki/Royal_and_noble_ranks_of_the_Qing_dynasty" title="Royal and noble ranks of the Qing dynasty">Royal and noble ranks of the Qing dynasty</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mandarin_(bureaucrat)#Ranks_under_the_Qing_dynasty" title="Mandarin (bureaucrat)">Mandarin (bureaucrat) §&#160;Ranks under the Qing dynasty</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Great_Qing_Legal_Code" title="Great Qing Legal Code">Great Qing Legal Code</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Qing_Dynasty_Mandarin.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Qing_Dynasty_Mandarin.jpg/170px-Qing_Dynasty_Mandarin.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="247" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Qing_Dynasty_Mandarin.jpg/255px-Qing_Dynasty_Mandarin.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Qing_Dynasty_Mandarin.jpg/340px-Qing_Dynasty_Mandarin.jpg 2x" data-file-width="399" data-file-height="580" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Qing_Dynasty_Mandarin.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Qing dynasty mandarin</div></div></div>
<p>The early Qing emperors adopted the <a href="/wiki/Bureaucratic" class="mw-redirect" title="Bureaucratic">bureaucratic</a> structures and institutions from the preceding <a href="/wiki/Ming_dynasty" title="Ming dynasty">Ming dynasty</a> but split rule between <a href="/wiki/Han_Chinese" title="Han Chinese">Han Chinese</a> and <a href="/wiki/Manchus" class="mw-redirect" title="Manchus">Manchus</a>, with some positions also given to <a href="/wiki/Mongols" title="Mongols">Mongols</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpence201239_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpence201239-118">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> Like previous dynasties, the Qing recruited officials via the <a href="/wiki/Imperial_examination" title="Imperial examination">imperial examination system</a>, until the system was abolished in 1905. The Qing divided the positions into civil and military positions, each having nine grades or ranks, each subdivided into a and b categories. Civil appointments ranged from an attendant to the emperor or a Grand Secretary in the Forbidden City (highest) to being a prefectural tax collector, deputy jail warden, deputy police commissioner, or tax examiner.  Military appointments ranged from being a field marshal or chamberlain of the imperial bodyguard to a third class sergeant, corporal or a first or second class private.<sup id="cite_ref-Jackson_&amp;_Hugus_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jackson_&amp;_Hugus-119">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Central_government_agencies">Central government agencies</span></h3>
<p>The formal structure of the Qing government centered on the Emperor as the absolute ruler, who presided over six Boards (Ministries<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;d&#93;</a></sup>), each headed by two presidents<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;e&#93;</a></sup> and assisted by four vice presidents.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;f&#93;</a></sup> In contrast to the Ming system, however, Qing ethnic policy dictated that appointments were split between Manchu noblemen and Han officials who had passed the highest levels of the <a href="/wiki/Imperial_examination" title="Imperial examination">state examinations</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Grand_Secretariat" title="Grand Secretariat">Grand Secretariat</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;g&#93;</a></sup> which had been an important policy-making body under the Ming, lost its importance during the Qing and evolved into an imperial <a href="/wiki/Chancery_(medieval_office)" title="Chancery (medieval office)">chancery</a>. The institutions which had been inherited from the Ming formed the core of the Qing "<a href="/w/index.php?title=Outer_Court&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Outer Court (page does not exist)">Outer Court</a>," which handled routine matters and was located in the southern part of the <a href="/wiki/Forbidden_City" title="Forbidden City">Forbidden City</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_emperor_of_China_from_%27The_Universal_Traveller%27.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/The_emperor_of_China_from_%27The_Universal_Traveller%27.jpg/170px-The_emperor_of_China_from_%27The_Universal_Traveller%27.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/The_emperor_of_China_from_%27The_Universal_Traveller%27.jpg/255px-The_emperor_of_China_from_%27The_Universal_Traveller%27.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/The_emperor_of_China_from_%27The_Universal_Traveller%27.jpg/340px-The_emperor_of_China_from_%27The_Universal_Traveller%27.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="1067" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_emperor_of_China_from_%27The_Universal_Traveller%27.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>The emperor of China from The Universal Traveller</i></div></div></div>
<p>In order not to let the routine administration take over the running of the empire, the Qing emperors made sure that all important matters were decided in the "<a href="/w/index.php?title=Inner_Court&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Inner Court (page does not exist)">Inner Court</a>," which was dominated by the imperial family and Manchu nobility and which was located in the northern part of the Forbidden City. The core institution of the inner court was the <a href="/wiki/Grand_Council_(Qing_dynasty)" title="Grand Council (Qing dynasty)">Grand Council</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;h&#93;</a></sup> It emerged in the 1720s under the reign of the <a href="/wiki/Yongzheng_Emperor" title="Yongzheng Emperor">Yongzheng Emperor</a> as a body charged with handling Qing military campaigns against the Mongols, but it soon took over other military and administrative duties and served to centralize authority under the crown.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBartlett1991_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBartlett1991-125">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> The Grand Councillors<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;i&#93;</a></sup> served as a sort of <a href="/wiki/Privy_council" title="Privy council">privy council</a> to the emperor.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Three_Departments_and_Six_Ministries" title="Three Departments and Six Ministries">Six Ministries</a> and their respective areas of responsibilities were as follows:
</p><p><b><a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Personnel" title="Ministry of Personnel">Board of Civil Appointments</a></b><sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;j&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>The personnel administration of all civil officials – including evaluation, promotion, and dismissal. It was also in charge of the "honours list".</dd></dl>
<p><b><a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Revenue_(imperial_China)" title="Ministry of Revenue (imperial China)">Board of Revenue</a></b><sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;k&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>The literal translation of the Chinese word <i>hu</i> (户) is "household". For much of Qing history, the government's main source of revenue came from taxation on landownership supplemented by <a href="/wiki/Salt_in_Chinese_History#Prosperity,_culture,_corruption,_reform_in_the_Qing_dynasty" class="mw-redirect" title="Salt in Chinese History">official monopolies on salt</a>, which was an essential household item, and tea. Thus, in the predominantly agrarian Qing dynasty, the "household" was the basis of imperial finance. The department was charged with revenue collection and the financial management of the government.</dd></dl>
<p><b><a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Rites" title="Ministry of Rites">Board of Rites</a></b><sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;l&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>This board was responsible for all matters concerning court protocol. It organized the periodic worship of ancestors and various gods by the emperor, managed relations with <a href="/wiki/List_of_tributaries_of_Imperial_China#Qing_dynasty" class="mw-redirect" title="List of tributaries of Imperial China">tributary nations</a>, and oversaw the nationwide <a href="/wiki/Imperial_examination" title="Imperial examination">civil examination system</a>.</dd></dl>
<p><b><a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_War_(imperial_China)" title="Ministry of War (imperial China)">Board of War</a></b><sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;m&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>Unlike its Ming predecessor, which had full control over all military matters, the Qing Board of War had very limited powers. First, the <a href="/wiki/Eight_Banners" title="Eight Banners">Eight Banners</a> were under the direct control of the emperor and hereditary Manchu and Mongol princes, leaving the ministry only with authority over the <a href="/wiki/Green_Standard_Army" title="Green Standard Army">Green Standard Army</a>. Furthermore, the ministry's functions were purely administrative. Campaigns and troop movements were monitored and directed by the emperor, first through the Manchu ruling council, and later through the Grand Council.</dd></dl>
<p><b><a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Justice_(imperial_China)" title="Ministry of Justice (imperial China)">Board of Punishments</a></b><sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;n&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>The Board of Punishments handled all legal matters, including the supervision of various law courts and prisons. The <a href="/wiki/Great_Qing_Legal_Code" title="Great Qing Legal Code">Qing legal framework</a> was relatively weak compared to modern day legal systems, as there was no separation of executive and legislative branches of government. The legal system could be inconsistent, and, at times, arbitrary, because the emperor ruled by decree and had final say on all judicial outcomes. Emperors could (and did) overturn judgements of lower courts from time to time. Fairness of treatment was also an issue under the system of control practised by the Manchu government over the Han Chinese majority. To counter these inadequacies and keep the population in line, the Qing government maintained a very harsh penal code towards the Han populace, but it was no more severe than previous Chinese dynasties.</dd></dl>
<p><b><a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Works_(imperial_China)" title="Ministry of Works (imperial China)">Board of Works</a></b><sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;o&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>The Board of Works handled all governmental building projects, including palaces, temples and the repairs of waterways and flood canals. It was also in charge of minting coinage.</dd></dl>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Qing_Dynasty-2000_wen-1859.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Qing_Dynasty-2000_wen-1859.jpg/170px-Qing_Dynasty-2000_wen-1859.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="297" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Qing_Dynasty-2000_wen-1859.jpg/255px-Qing_Dynasty-2000_wen-1859.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Qing_Dynasty-2000_wen-1859.jpg/340px-Qing_Dynasty-2000_wen-1859.jpg 2x" data-file-width="372" data-file-height="650" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Qing_Dynasty-2000_wen-1859.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>2000–<a href="/wiki/Chinese_cash_(currency)" class="mw-redirect" title="Chinese cash (currency)">cash</a> <a href="/wiki/Da-Qing_Baochao" title="Da-Qing Baochao">Da-Qing Baochao</a> banknote from 1859</div></div></div>
<p>From the early Qing, the central government was characterized by a system of dual appointments by which each position in the central government had a Manchu and a Han Chinese assigned to it. The Han Chinese appointee was required to do the substantive work and the Manchu to ensure Han loyalty to Qing rule.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> The distinction between Han Chinese and Manchus extended to their court costumes. During the <a href="/wiki/Qianlong_Emperor" title="Qianlong Emperor">Qianlong Emperor</a>'s reign, for example, members of his family were distinguished by garments with a small circular emblem on the back, whereas Han officials wore clothing with a square emblem.
</p><p>In addition to the six boards, there was a <a href="/wiki/Lifan_Yuan" title="Lifan Yuan">Lifan Yuan</a><sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;p&#93;</a></sup> unique to the Qing government. This institution was established to supervise the administration of Tibet and the Mongol lands. As the empire expanded, it took over administrative responsibility of all minority ethnic groups living in and around the empire, including early contacts with Russia – then seen as a tribute nation. The office had the status of a full ministry and was headed by officials of equal rank. However, appointees were at first restricted only to candidates of Manchu and Mongol ethnicity, until later open to Han Chinese as well.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Yantai_(Chefoo),_Qing_Dynasty_postage_stamp.gif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Yantai_%28Chefoo%29%2C_Qing_Dynasty_postage_stamp.gif/170px-Yantai_%28Chefoo%29%2C_Qing_Dynasty_postage_stamp.gif" decoding="async" width="170" height="187" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/bb/Yantai_%28Chefoo%29%2C_Qing_Dynasty_postage_stamp.gif 1.5x" data-file-width="185" data-file-height="203" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Yantai_(Chefoo),_Qing_Dynasty_postage_stamp.gif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A postage stamp from <a href="/wiki/Yantai" title="Yantai">Yantai</a> (Chefoo) in the Qing dynasty</div></div></div>
<p>Even though the Board of Rites and Lifan Yuan performed some duties of a foreign office, they fell short of developing into a professional foreign service. It was not until 1861 – a year after losing the <a href="/wiki/Second_Opium_War" title="Second Opium War">Second Opium War</a> to the Anglo-French coalition – that the Qing government bowed to foreign pressure and created a proper foreign affairs office known as the <a href="/wiki/Zongli_Yamen" title="Zongli Yamen">Zongli Yamen</a>. The office was originally intended to be temporary and was staffed by officials seconded from the Grand Council. However, as dealings with foreigners became increasingly complicated and frequent, the office grew in size and importance, aided by revenue from customs duties which came under its direct jurisdiction.
</p><p>There was also another government institution called <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Household_Department" title="Imperial Household Department">Imperial Household Department</a> which was unique to the Qing dynasty. It was established before the fall of the Ming, but it became mature only after 1661, following the death of the <a href="/wiki/Shunzhi_Emperor" title="Shunzhi Emperor">Shunzhi Emperor</a> and the accession of his son, the <a href="/wiki/Kangxi_Emperor" title="Kangxi Emperor">Kangxi Emperor</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERawski1998179_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERawski1998179-135">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> The department's original purpose was to manage the internal affairs of the imperial family and the activities of the <a href="/wiki/Forbidden_City#Inner_Court" title="Forbidden City">inner palace</a> (in which tasks it largely replaced <a href="/wiki/Eunuch#China" title="Eunuch">eunuchs</a>), but it also played an important role in Qing relations with <a href="/wiki/History_of_Tibet#Khoshuts,_Zunghars,_and_Manchus" title="History of Tibet">Tibet</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mongolia_during_Qing_rule" class="mw-redirect" title="Mongolia during Qing rule">Mongolia</a>, engaged in trading activities (jade, <a href="/wiki/Ginseng" title="Ginseng">ginseng</a>, salt, furs, etc.), managed textile factories in the <a href="/wiki/Jiangnan" title="Jiangnan">Jiangnan</a> region, and even published books.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERawski1998179–180_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERawski1998179–180-136">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Salt_in_Chinese_History#Prosperity,_culture,_corruption,_reform_in_the_Qing_dynasty" class="mw-redirect" title="Salt in Chinese History">Relations with the Salt Superintendents and salt merchants</a>, such as those at Yangzhou, were particularly lucrative, especially since they were direct, and did not go through absorptive layers of bureaucracy.  The department was manned by <i><a href="/wiki/Booi_Aha" title="Booi Aha">booi</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;q&#93;</a></sup> or "bondservants," from the Upper Three <a href="/wiki/Eight_Banners" title="Eight Banners">Banners</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETorbert197727_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETorbert197727-138">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> By the 19th century, it managed the activities of at least 56 subagencies.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERawski1998179_135-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERawski1998179-135">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETorbert197728_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETorbert197728-139">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Administrative_divisions">Administrative divisions</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_administrative_divisions_of_China_before_1912#Provinces_and_Feudatory_Regions_under_the_Qing_dynasty" title="History of the administrative divisions of China before 1912">History of the administrative divisions of China before 1912 §&#160;Provinces and Feudatory Regions under the Qing dynasty</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Qing_dynasty_in_Inner_Asia" title="Qing dynasty in Inner Asia">Qing dynasty in Inner Asia</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Qing_Empire_circa_1820_EN.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Qing_Empire_circa_1820_EN.svg/280px-Qing_Empire_circa_1820_EN.svg.png" decoding="async" width="280" height="245" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Qing_Empire_circa_1820_EN.svg/420px-Qing_Empire_circa_1820_EN.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Qing_Empire_circa_1820_EN.svg/560px-Qing_Empire_circa_1820_EN.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1172" data-file-height="1024" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Qing_Empire_circa_1820_EN.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Qing Empire ca. 1820</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Qing_Dynasty_1820.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Qing_Dynasty_1820.png/280px-Qing_Dynasty_1820.png" decoding="async" width="280" height="208" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Qing_Dynasty_1820.png/420px-Qing_Dynasty_1820.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Qing_Dynasty_1820.png/560px-Qing_Dynasty_1820.png 2x" data-file-width="910" data-file-height="675" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Qing_Dynasty_1820.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Qing dynasty in 1820, with provinces in yellow, military governorates and protectorates in light yellow, tributary states in orange</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Taiping2.PNG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Taiping2.PNG/280px-Taiping2.PNG" decoding="async" width="280" height="208" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Taiping2.PNG/420px-Taiping2.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Taiping2.PNG/560px-Taiping2.PNG 2x" data-file-width="910" data-file-height="675" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Taiping2.PNG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Qing dynasty in 1854</div></div></div>
<p>Qing China reached its largest extent during the 18th century, when it ruled <a href="/wiki/China_proper" title="China proper">China proper</a> (eighteen provinces) as well as the areas of present-day <a href="/wiki/Northeast_China" title="Northeast China">Northeast China</a>, <a href="/wiki/Inner_Mongolia" title="Inner Mongolia">Inner Mongolia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Outer_Mongolia" title="Outer Mongolia">Outer Mongolia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Xinjiang" title="Xinjiang">Xinjiang</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tibet" title="Tibet">Tibet</a>, at approximately 13 million km<sup>2</sup> in size. There were originally 18 provinces, all of which in China proper, but later this number was increased to 22, with Manchuria and Xinjiang being divided or turned into provinces. <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Taiwan</a>, originally part of <a href="/wiki/Fujian" title="Fujian">Fujian</a> province, became a province of its own in the 19th century, but was ceded to the <a href="/wiki/Empire_of_Japan" title="Empire of Japan">Empire of Japan</a> following the <a href="/wiki/First_Sino-Japanese_War" title="First Sino-Japanese War">First Sino-Japanese War</a> by the end of the century. In addition, many surrounding countries, such as <a href="/wiki/Korea" title="Korea">Korea</a> (<a href="/wiki/Joseon" title="Joseon">Joseon dynasty</a>), <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a> frequently <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Chinese_tributary_system" class="mw-redirect" title="Imperial Chinese tributary system">paid tribute</a> to China during much of this period. The <a href="/wiki/Katoor_dynasty" title="Katoor dynasty">Katoor dynasty</a> of Afghanistan also paid tribute to the Qing dynasty of China until the mid-19th century.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources#What_information_to_include" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="A complete citation is needed (May 2019)">full citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> During the Qing dynasty the Chinese claimed suzerainty over the <a href="/wiki/Taghdumbash_Pamir" title="Taghdumbash Pamir">Taghdumbash Pamir</a> in the south-west of <a href="/wiki/Taxkorgan_Tajik_Autonomous_County" title="Taxkorgan Tajik Autonomous County">Taxkorgan Tajik Autonomous County</a> but permitted the <a href="/wiki/Mir_of_Hunza" title="Mir of Hunza">Mir of Hunza</a> to administer the region in return for a <a href="/wiki/List_of_tributaries_of_Imperial_China" class="mw-redirect" title="List of tributaries of Imperial China">tribute</a>.  Until 1937 the inhabitants paid tribute to the <a href="/wiki/Mir_of_Hunza" title="Mir of Hunza">Mir of Hunza</a>, who exercised control over the pastures.<sup id="cite_ref-Kreutzmann_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kreutzmann-141">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Khanate_of_Kokand" title="Khanate of Kokand">Khanate of Kokand</a> were forced to submit as <a href="/wiki/Protectorate" title="Protectorate">protectorate</a> and pay tribute to the Qing dynasty in China between 1774 and 1798.
</p>
